Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Need for an Islamic Educational Theory
Author introduces the importance of Quranic epistemology for Islamic education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the general-to-specific relationship between educational theory and epistemology and identify the ideological motivation for developing an Islamic educational theory.

Core Idea
Islamic education needs its own epistemological foundation to preserve identity and counter Western ideological influence.

This opening passage sets the stage for the entire study. The author argues that the contemporary Muslim researcher must articulate an Islamic educational theory rooted in Quranic epistemology, replacing Western theories that fragment the youth's identity. The key insight is the hierarchical relationship between educational theory and the theory of knowledge: the former is a specific application of the latter. Understanding this relationship is crucial for building a coherent Islamic educational system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central concern
Read the first sentence: the author is concerned with how Islamic education approaches knowledge and how it can be applied today. The goal is to replace Western theories with an authentic Islamic one.
2
Recognize the ideological conflict
The passage mentions 'صراع الإيديولوجيات' (conflict of ideologies) in education. This conflict forces the Muslim researcher to present an Islamic educational theory that preserves civilizational and doctrinal identity.
3
Determine the relationship between educational theory and epistemology
The author states that the relationship is 'العام بالخاص' (general to specific) or 'الكل بالجزء' (whole to part). Educational theory is a specific branch of the broader theory of knowledge.
This is a key methodological point: any educational system implicitly relies on an epistemology, so building an Islamic education requires first grounding it in an Islamic theory of knowledge.

Introduction: However, the matter that concerns the Muslim researcher today is - first and foremost - the vision of the philosophy of Islamic education regarding the issue of knowledge from the previous angles, and how it can reflect on the contemporary reality of Islamic education in a way that meets the need of the contemporary Muslim and the contemporary Muslim society, in replacing the elements of the desired Islamic educational theory with the Western educational theories that scatter the minds of the youth and hinder their launching from their civilizational self and their doctrinal identity. Indeed, the conflict of ideologies in the field of education in particular necessitates that the Muslim researcher strive to present his Islamic educational theory in various aspects, including the aspect of knowledge, due to its importance stemming from it being the embodiment of his civilizational stance in the foundations of education and its consequent effects on educational reality. That is because the relationship between educational theory and the theory of knowledge is a relationship of the general to the specific, or the whole to the part, as will be indicated in its appropriate place in this study.

Commentary
This passage is a general introduction under the heading 'مقدمة' (Introduction). The author begins by stating the primary concern of the contemporary Muslim researcher: to understand how the philosophy of Islamic education approaches the issue of knowledge and to apply that understanding to modern educational reality. The goal is to replace Western educational theories with an authentic Islamic educational theory that preserves the youth's civilizational identity and doctrinal foundation. The author emphasizes that the struggle of ideologies in education compels the Muslim researcher to articulate an Islamic educational theory, and within it, a theory of knowledge, which is central to the educational enterprise. Finally, the author notes that the relationship between educational theory and epistemology is one of general to specific or whole to part, a point that will be elaborated later in the study.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Foundational Premise of Quranic Knowledge
Author states the main premise of the study regarding Quranic knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four attributes of Quranic knowledge as stated in the study's premise and explain their significance for deriving an epistemological theory.

Core Idea
Quranic knowledge is comprehensive, integrated, eternal, and absolute.

Every research study begins with a premise (musallama). In this source, the author explicitly lays out the foundational assumption on which the entire investigation rests: that Quranic knowledge possesses four distinct attributes. Understanding these attributes is essential because they justify the project of extracting an epistemological theory from the Quran. Let's examine the premise closely and see how it shapes the research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise (musallama)
Find where the author states the main assumption of the study. The passage begins with 'مسلمة الدراسة' (Premise of the study). This signals that what follows is the foundational claim accepted without proof within the research.
In logic, a musallama is a proposition conceded for argument; here it is a methodological postulate.
2
Extract the four attributes
Look for the four adjectives describing Quranic knowledge: الشمول (comprehensiveness), التكامل (integration), الخلود (eternity), الإطلاقية (absoluteness). Note that they are listed in a sequence after the verb تتسم.
These attributes are presented as inherent characteristics of Quranic knowledge.
3
Understand the consequence
Read the phrase that follows: 'على نحو يدفع الباحث للعمل على استنطاق نصوص الوحي الإلهي' (in a manner that drives the researcher to work on eliciting the texts of divine revelation). The four attributes are not just descriptive; they provide the motivation and justification for the entire research project of deriving an epistemological theory.
The premise thus has a methodological function: it justifies the Quran as a source for a contemporary educational theory.

This passage is labeled 'مسلمة الدراسة' (Premise of the Study). The author explicitly states the foundational assumption upon which the entire study is built: that Quranic knowledge possesses four attributes: comprehensiveness (شمول), integration (تكامل), eternity (خلود), and absoluteness (إطلاقية). Because of these qualities, the researcher is motivated to interrogate the Quranic texts to derive an epistemological theory for Islamic education. This theory is intended to fulfill the needs of Islamic education and help shape its contemporary formulation.

Identifying Research Gaps in Islamic Educational Studies
Author explains why he chose this topic and highlights its importance for Islamic education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a research justification in Islamic academic writing and evaluate its completeness.

Core Idea
A strong research justification identifies a clear gap, a source of authority (Quran), and a practical application.

The author explains why he chose to study Quranic epistemology for education. This is not a logical proof but a procedural justification. As a scholar, you must be able to recognize the components of such a justification and assess whether it convincingly demonstrates the need for your research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central problem
Look for the statement that defines what is missing or lacking in existing research.
In this passage, the problem is the absence of academic studies dedicated to Quranic educational epistemology.
2
Find the source of authority
Determine the primary Islamic source the study anchors itself to (e.g., Quran, Hadith).
The author explicitly states the Quran as the starting point.
3
Check the practical application
Identify the field where the findings will be applied (e.g., education, fiqh, tafsir).
Here, the field is Islamic education, with a focus on deriving educational implications.
4
Evaluate completeness
Assess whether the justification addresses both the theoretical gap and the practical need, and whether it responds to calls from scholars in the field.
The author cites philosophers of Islamic education and aims to rectify both the gap and the lack of applied research.

Objectives of an Islamic Epistemology Study
Author lists the two main objectives of the research.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two main objectives of an Islamic epistemology study and explain how they differ from classical mantiq.

Core Idea
Quranic epistemology expands classical mantiq from logical forms to the nature and educational dimensions of knowledge.

This short passage from the research introduction outlines two clear objectives: first, to survey the development and vocabulary of general epistemology; second, to investigate the nature of knowledge as presented in the Quran and its educational implications. These objectives signal a departure from classical mantiq, which focused on formal logical structures. Understanding this shift is crucial for situating the study within Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objectives
Read the Arabic under 'أهداف الدراسة' and note the two numbered points. The first refers to general epistemology, the second to Quranic knowledge and its educational aspects.
The second objective explicitly includes 'جوانب تربوية' (educational aspects), which is absent in classical mantiq.
2
Contrast with classical mantiq's scope
Recall that classical mantiq primarily analyzes argument forms, terms, and syllogisms. Here, the study aims to explore the content and application of knowledge, not just its form.
This reflects a shift from 'ilm al-mantiq (logic) to 'ilm al-ma'rifa (epistemology).
3
Recognize the educational dimension
The second objective emphasizes 'جوانب تربوية' (educational aspects). This is a key point: Quranic knowledge is not just theoretical but has practical educational implications.
This connects to Islamic pedagogy and tarbiya.

First, the study will examine epistemology in general, tracing its historical development and defining key terms relevant to the study. Second, it will investigate the nature of knowledge as presented in the Quran and identify the educational implications inherent in that concept. These objectives set the scope for the subsequent chapters.

Bridging Quran and Islamic Education
Author explains rationale and goals of the study.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline a research problem statement for an Islamic study, identifying gaps and objectives.

Core Idea
Identifying a clear research gap is the first step in any Islamic theory-building.

This passage shows how a researcher identifies a gap in existing studies and sets a clear scope. You will learn to recognize this structuring and apply it to your own research in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the research gap
Find where the author states what existing works lack. They mention studies on theory of knowledge in Islam that either omit the Quran or do not link to education.
The gap is between Quran-focused studies and educational application.
2
Determine the proposed solution
The author proposes taking the Quran as starting point and applying it to Islamic education. See how this bridges the gap.
3
List the study's objectives
The passage lists four objectives: general theory of knowledge, nature of knowledge in Quran, sources/methods, and possibility of knowledge. Each has educational aspects.
Objectives are derived from the gap.
4
Relate objectives to overall contribution
The first importance statement says the study contributes to crystallizing contemporary Islamic educational theory. See how the objectives feed into this.
This shows the larger purpose.

Methodology of the study: This study follows the fundamentalist methodology based on description and deduction. One researcher defines the fundamentalist methodology as that methodology followed by Muslim scholars in formulating their ideas, culture, and behaviors according to the universal legal principles, so that they take the religious character that derives its legitimacy from the doctrine of monotheism. Another researcher specified the meaning of studying educational topics according to this methodology, saying: They are those studies that tend to deduce and extract the theoretical principles and foundations of Islamic education and its intellectual framework and what is connected to it of educational and instructional goals and values through studying the Holy Quran and the purified Sunnah as the two main sources, and what revolves around them of scholars' efforts. Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to a modern research study titled 'Nazariyya Quran' (Quranic Theory). It begins by explaining the rationale for the study: existing works on theory of knowledge in Islam either did not focus specifically on the Quran or did not connect to Islamic education. The author aims to fill this gap by taking the Quran as the starting point and applying its insights to Islamic education. The importance of the study is then stated: first, to contribute to the development of contemporary Islamic educational theory by studying the Quranic theory of knowledge; second, to derive educational implications that can improve the educational system. The objectives are listed: to examine the theory of knowledge in general, the nature of knowledge in the Quran and its educational aspects, the sources and methods of knowledge in the Quran and their educational aspects, and the possibility of obtaining knowledge in the Quran and its educational aspects. The methodology is described as 'al-manhaj al-usuli' (fundamentalist/original methodology), which is based on description (wasf) and deduction (istinbat). This methodology is defined by citing other researchers: it is the method used by Muslim scholars to formulate their ideas and culture according to universal legal principles, deriving legitimacy from tawhid. It involves deducing the theoretical foundations of Islamic education from the Quran and Sunnah. Finally, the procedures are summarized: the core material is the Quran, so the author reads relevant verses in their objective context. The passage sets the stage for the rest of the study, establishing its scope, method, and goals.

Key Terms
المنهج الأصولي (al-manhaj al-usuli): The methodology based on description and deduction, used by Islamic scholars to formulate ideas according to universal legal principles, deriving legitimacy from tawhid. [Note: In classical usul al-fiqh, 'al-manhaj al-usuli' refers to the methodology of deriving legal rulings; here it is adapted for educational theory.]
التضمينات التربوية (al-tadmeenat al-tarbawiyya): Educational implications or applications derived from the theoretical cognitive framework.
الوصف والاستنباط (al-wasf wa al-istinbat): Description and deduction; description refers to examining the text, and deduction refers to extracting principles and implications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To compare Quranic knowledge with Greek philosophy.
D) To evaluate modern educational systems.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the starting point for deriving an Islamic theory of knowledge applicable to education.

The Fundamentalist Methodology: Description and Deduction
Author defines the fundamentalist methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-manhaj al-usuli and apply its two components (wasf and istinbat) to analyze a Quranic passage.

Core Idea
Al-manhaj al-usuli is the classical method of describing revealed texts and deducing principles, rooted in tawhid.

The passage introduces and defines 'al-manhaj al-usuli' (fundamentalist/original methodology) as the approach for this study. This methodology, central to Islamic scholarship, involves describing the primary sources and then deducing principles. We will dissect this definition and see how it functions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of al-manhaj al-usuli
Find where the passage defines this methodology. It is described as a method that Muslim scholars used to formulate ideas according to universal legal principles, deriving legitimacy from tawhid.
Look for the phrase 'المنهج الأصولي' and the cited definition.
2
Identify its two main processes
The passage says the methodology is based on 'al-wasf wa al-istinbat' (description and deduction). Description involves examining the text; deduction involves extracting principles.
These two processes are complementary.
3
Understand the source of legitimacy
According to the cited researcher, this methodology derives its religious character from the doctrine of tawhid. This gives it authority in Islamic scholarship.
Tawhid is the foundation.
4
See its application to education
Another researcher explains that studying educational topics using this methodology means deducing the theoretical foundations of Islamic education from the Quran and Sunnah.
This connects the method to the study's field.
5
Assess the definition's completeness
Consider whether this definition includes all elements of Islamic legal methodology, such as ijma' and qiyas. The passage focuses on Quran and Sunnah, but usul al-fiqh also includes other sources.
This is a simplified definition for educational studies.

So the starting point of the study is the Holy Quran, and the field of its application is Islamic education. And in that is a response and interaction with the call of some philosophers of Islamic education to build a theory of knowledge whose foundation is taken from the Holy Quran while linking it with appropriate educational applications. Importance of the study: The importance of the study lies in the following: 1 - Striving to contribute to embodying one of the features of the contemporary Islamic educational theory, through studying the theory of knowledge in the Holy Quran to contribute to crystallizing its general theory. - Striving to apply the theoretical cognitive framework, and deduce the educational implications that can develop the reality of the elements of the educational system. Objectives of the study: The study seeks to uncover the following topics: 1- The theory of knowledge - in general - in terms of its development and introduction to its vocabulary related to the subject of the study. 2 - The nature of knowledge in the Holy Quran, and what it includes of educational aspects. Introduction - Sources of knowledge and its methods in the Holy Quran, and what they include of educational aspects. 4 - The possibility of obtaining knowledge in the Holy Quran, and what it includes of educational aspects. Methodology of the study: This study follows the fundamentalist methodology based on description and deduction. One researcher defines the fundamentalist methodology as that methodology followed by Muslim scholars in formulating their ideas, culture, and behaviors according to the universal legal principles, so that they take the religious character that derives its legitimacy from the doctrine of monotheism. Another researcher specified the meaning of studying educational topics according to this methodology, saying: They are those studies that tend to deduce and extract the theoretical principles and foundations of Islamic education and its intellectual framework and what is connected to it of educational and instructional goals and values through studying the Holy Quran and the purified Sunnah as the two main sources, and what revolves around them of scholars' efforts. Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to a modern research study titled 'Nazariyya Quran' (Quranic Theory). It begins by explaining the rationale for the study: existing works on theory of knowledge in Islam either did not focus specifically on the Quran or did not connect to Islamic education. The author aims to fill this gap by taking the Quran as the starting point and applying its insights to Islamic education. The importance of the study is then stated: first, to contribute to the development of contemporary Islamic educational theory by studying the Quranic theory of knowledge; second, to derive educational implications that can improve the educational system. The objectives are listed: to examine the theory of knowledge in general, the nature of knowledge in the Quran and its educational aspects, the sources and methods of knowledge in the Quran and their educational aspects, and the possibility of obtaining knowledge in the Quran and its educational aspects. The methodology is described as 'al-manhaj al-usuli' (fundamentalist/original methodology), which is based on description (wasf) and deduction (istinbat). This methodology is defined by citing other researchers: it is the method used by Muslim scholars to formulate their ideas and culture according to universal legal principles, deriving legitimacy from tawhid. It involves deducing the theoretical foundations of Islamic education from the Quran and Sunnah. Finally, the procedures are summarized: the core material is the Quran, so the author reads relevant verses in their objective context. The passage sets the stage for the rest of the study, establishing its scope, method, and goals.

Key Terms
المنهج الأصولي (al-manhaj al-usuli): The methodology based on description and deduction, used by Islamic scholars to formulate ideas according to universal legal principles, deriving legitimacy from tawhid. [Note: In classical usul al-fiqh, 'al-manhaj al-usuli' refers to the methodology of deriving legal rulings; here it is adapted for educational theory.]
الوصف والاستنباط (al-wasf wa al-istinbat): Description and deduction; description refers to examining the text, and deduction refers to extracting principles and implications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'al-manhaj al-usuli' derive its legitimacy from, according to the passage?
A) Rational consensus.
B) The doctrine of tawhid.
C) Empirical evidence.
D) Traditional practice.

Key Takeaway
Al-manhaj al-usuli is the method of describing primary sources and deducing principles, rooted in tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the method of description and deduction, read Quran 96:1-5 and deduce one educational implication. Describe your steps: first describe what the verses say (wasf), then deduce a principle for education (istinbat).

Expert Validation Methodology in Quranic Research
Author details his research methodology for collecting and verifying Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the steps the author took to validate Quranic verse citations using an expert committee.

Core Idea
Expert committee validation ensures accuracy in Quranic research.

The author spent a full year collecting verses and created a 60-page list. To ensure accuracy, he submitted this list to a panel of fifteen experts in Tafsir and Islamic education. The varied responses led him to a key conclusion about the necessity of returning to tafsir sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the research need
The author needed to ensure accurate citation and interpretation of Quranic verses for his educational study.
2
Prepare the list
A 60-page list was created including surah name, verse number, text, and citation accuracy.
The list was submitted to a committee of fifteen experts.
3
Analyze expert responses
Responses varied: no reply, promises, returns, minor corrections, and a few thorough evaluations.
Emphasize that only a small number provided detailed feedback.
4
Draw the conclusion
The author concluded that returning to authoritative tafsir books was inevitable for proper interpretation.
This decision was reinforced by the consensus of the arbitrators.

that occurred in it, and this effort took me about a full year, collecting and classifying, without relying at all on the traditional lexicographical method.
- Preparing a list of derived verses consisting of 60 pages (Folscap) including the name of the surah, the verse number, the text of the verse under citation, and the accuracy of the citation attached (a sample at the end of the thesis), with the aim of submitting it to a committee of experts for arbitration regarding the accuracy of the researcher's citation and the correctness of his direction of any of those texts, based on his knowledge background of the Noble Quran and the foundations of Islamic education, due to his specialization in previous stages and his personal interest in the issue of knowledge in general, and knowledge in Islam in particular. This came from the premise that the direction of the exegetical sources differs between ancient and modern, both in terms of the purpose of the work, the style of presentation, or the method of directing the texts, meaning that directed purposeful educational inference cannot be realized in any of those sources - despite their importance - to meet the objectives of this study, and therefore I resorted to the method of a reviewed list by a committee of experts, consisting of fifteen professors in Tafsir and Islamic education - if they specialized - or had a prominent interest in it. It became clear after a not-so-short time after submitting the set of lists to about twelve professors inside and outside Yemen, varied reactions as follows: - No response received from inside Yemen from a large portion of those professors despite continuous follow-up by various means.
- Promises of response, but they did not arrive until the time of writing these lines.
- Returning the list exactly as it was, with an apology on the grounds of lack of specialization or busyness.
- Returning it with corrections that are not noteworthy.

Introduction
- A very small group, constituting an insignificant number from the group of professors inside the country, read the list thoroughly and gave a number of notes, most of which I benefited from in the exegetical aspects at that time.
- Some responses from outside from one of the most prominent professors of Foundations of Education gave a strong impetus to this work, where the list was studied carefully, and some valuable directions were provided, with warm praise, perhaps as encouragement to continue the work, and others - from outside professors - sufficed with giving general directions about the list without entering into details about the accuracy of the citation.
-
The opinions of the arbitrators almost agree - whether those who interacted (and they are an insignificant few), or those who apologized and returned the list, or those who were still promising good until the time of this writing, or those who responded to an invitation made by the researcher in cooperation with one of his supervisors to hold a seminar to give main notes on the lists submitted to them in advance - almost all those opinions agreed on the necessity of returning to the exegetical sources, especially the well-known ones, and benefiting from that with the researcher's skill and good handling of the exegetical texts related to the subject of the study, which do not carry intended educational implications.
-
As a result of all the above, it was confirmed to me that returning to the books of tafsir is inevitable, and therefore I began to search for the meaning of each verse that I had cited in the aforementioned list, if its meaning needed an authoritative interpretation; to record that interpretation for this or that exegete.
- Reliance was placed on two principles: "Every general remains on its generality until something specifies it" and "The lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the particularity of the reason."
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction in which the author explains how he gathered and validated Quranic verses for his study on educational implications. The author begins by stating that he spent about a year collecting and classifying verses, deliberately avoiding the traditional lexicographical approach. He then describes creating a 60-page list of verses, including surah, verse number, text, and citation accuracy, which he submitted to a committee of fifteen experts specializing in Tafsir and Islamic education. His premise was that ancient and modern exegetical sources differ in purpose, style, and method, so a directed educational inference could not rely on any single source. The responses from the experts varied: many inside Yemen did not reply despite follow-ups; some gave promises that never materialized; others returned the list unchanged or with minor corrections. A small number of internal experts provided thorough feedback that benefited the author's exegetical understanding. External experts, notably one prominent professor of Foundations of Education, gave strong encouragement and valuable guidance, while others offered only general comments. The author concludes that the consensus among the arbitrators was the need to return to well-known exegetical sources and to skillfully handle texts that do not have explicit educational implications. Consequently, the author decided it was necessary to consult tafsir books to ascertain the meanings of each cited verse. Finally, he adopted two principles of Quranic interpretation: 'Every general remains general until something specifies it' and 'The lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the particularity of the occasion.' These principles are crucial in deriving educational lessons from Quranic verses without being limited to the specific circumstances of revelation.

Introduction and Front Matter

Principles of General and Special in Quranic Interpretation
Author details his research methodology for collecting and verifying Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle 'Every general remains general until something specifies it' to determine the scope of a Quranic ruling.

Core Idea
General Quranic statements retain their generality unless a specifying text limits them.

After receiving feedback from experts, the author adopted two foundational principles of usul al-fiqh to guide his interpretation of Quranic verses. These principles ensure that the educational lessons derived from the Quran are based on the general wording, not limited to specific occasions of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify a general verse
Find a Quranic verse that uses a general term, e.g., 'The thief, male and female, cut off their hands' (5:38) – the term 'thief' is general.
General terms include every, all, whoever, etc.
2
Search for specifying texts
Look for any other Quranic verse or hadith that limits the generality, e.g., the verse on repentance (5:39) may specify the punishment under certain conditions.
If no specifier exists, the ruling remains general.
3
Apply the first principle
If no specifying text is found, the ruling applies to all cases covered by the general term.
This principle is 'kullu 'āmin yabqā 'alā 'umūmihi ḥattā ya'tī mā yukhaṣṣiṣuhu'.
4
Apply the second principle
The lesson is taken from the general wording, not from the specific occasion of revelation. So even if the verse was revealed for a specific incident, its ruling applies generally.
This is 'al-'ibrah bi-'umūm al-lafẓ lā bi-khuṣūṣ al-sabab'.

- شكلت بعض ردود الخارج من أحد أبرز أساتذة أصول التربية دفعة قوية لهذا العمل، حيث تم دراسة القائمة بإمعان، وقدّمت بعض التوجيهات القيمة، مع ثناء عاطر ، لعله من قبيل التحفيز على مواصلة العمل واكتفى آخرون - من أساتذة الخارج - بإبداء توجيهات عامة حول القائمة دون الدخول في تفصيلات حول مدى دقة الاستشهاد.
-
تكاد تجمع آراء المحكمين سواء أولئك الذين تفاعلوا وهم قلة لا تذكر، أو الذين اعتذروا وأعادوا القائمة أو الذين ما يزالون يعدون خيراً حتى زمن الكتابة هذا، أو أولئك الذين لبوا دعوة وجهها الباحث بالتعاون مع أحد مشرفيه إلى عقد ندوة لإبداء الملحوظات الرئيسة على القوائم المسلّمة لهم سلفا، تكاد تجمع تلك الآراء على ضرورة الرجوع إلى المصادر التفسيرية والمشهور منها على وجه الخصوص، والإفادة من ذلك بمهارة الباحث، وحسن التعامل مع النصوص التفسيرية ذات العلاقة بموضوع الدراسة، التي لا تحمل دلالات تربوية مقصودة.
-
نتيجة لكل ما سبق تأكد لي أن العودة إلى كتب التفاسير أمر لا مناص منه، وعلى ذلك طفقت أبحث عن دلالة كل آية كنت قد استشهدت بها في القائمة المشار إليها، إذا كانت دلالتها بحاجة إلى تفسير معتمد؛ لأثبت ذلك التفسير لهذا المفسر أو ذاك.

يخصصه))، و((العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب.

that occurred in it, and this effort took me about a full year, collecting and classifying, without relying at all on the traditional lexicographical method.
- Preparing a list of derived verses consisting of 60 pages (Folscap) including the name of the surah, the verse number, the text of the verse under citation, and the accuracy of the citation attached (a sample at the end of the thesis), with the aim of submitting it to a committee of experts for arbitration regarding the accuracy of the researcher's citation and the correctness of his direction of any of those texts, based on his knowledge background of the Noble Quran and the foundations of Islamic education, due to his specialization in previous stages and his personal interest in the issue of knowledge in general, and knowledge in Islam in particular. This came from the premise that the direction of the exegetical sources differs between ancient and modern, both in terms of the purpose of the work, the style of presentation, or the method of directing the texts, meaning that directed purposeful educational inference cannot be realized in any of those sources - despite their importance - to meet the objectives of this study, and therefore I resorted to the method of a reviewed list by a committee of experts, consisting of fifteen professors in Tafsir and Islamic education - if they specialized - or had a prominent interest in it. It became clear after a not-so-short time after submitting the set of lists to about twelve professors inside and outside Yemen, varied reactions as follows: - No response received from inside Yemen from a large portion of those professors despite continuous follow-up by various means.
- Promises of response, but they did not arrive until the time of writing these lines.
- Returning the list exactly as it was, with an apology on the grounds of lack of specialization or busyness.
- Returning it with corrections that are not noteworthy.

[PAGE_032]
28
Introduction
- A very small group, constituting an insignificant number from the group of professors inside the country, read the list thoroughly and gave a number of notes, most of which I benefited from in the exegetical aspects at that time.
- Some responses from outside from one of the most prominent professors of Foundations of Education gave a strong impetus to this work, where the list was studied carefully, and some valuable directions were provided, with warm praise, perhaps as encouragement to continue the work, and others - from outside professors - sufficed with giving general directions about the list without entering into details about the accuracy of the citation.
-
The opinions of the arbitrators almost agree - whether those who interacted (and they are an insignificant few), or those who apologized and returned the list, or those who were still promising good until the time of this writing, or those who responded to an invitation made by the researcher in cooperation with one of his supervisors to hold a seminar to give main notes on the lists submitted to them in advance - almost all those opinions agreed on the necessity of returning to the exegetical sources, especially the well-known ones, and benefiting from that with the researcher's skill and good handling of the exegetical texts related to the subject of the study, which do not carry intended educational implications.
-
As a result of all the above, it was confirmed to me that returning to the books of tafsir is inevitable, and therefore I began to search for the meaning of each verse that I had cited in the aforementioned list, if its meaning needed an authoritative interpretation; to record that interpretation for this or that exegete.
- Reliance was placed on two principles: "Every general remains on its generality until something specifies it" and "The lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the particularity of the reason."
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction in which the author explains how he gathered and validated Quranic verses for his study on educational implications. The author begins by stating that he spent about a year collecting and classifying verses, deliberately avoiding the traditional lexicographical approach. He then describes creating a 60-page list of verses, including surah, verse number, text, and citation accuracy, which he submitted to a committee of fifteen experts specializing in Tafsir and Islamic education. His premise was that ancient and modern exegetical sources differ in purpose, style, and method, so a directed educational inference could not rely on any single source. The responses from the experts varied: many inside Yemen did not reply despite follow-ups; some gave promises that never materialized; others returned the list unchanged or with minor corrections. A small number of internal experts provided thorough feedback that benefited the author's exegetical understanding. External experts, notably one prominent professor of Foundations of Education, gave strong encouragement and valuable guidance, while others offered only general comments. The author concludes that the consensus among the arbitrators was the need to return to well-known exegetical sources and to skillfully handle texts that do not have explicit educational implications. Consequently, the author decided it was necessary to consult tafsir books to ascertain the meanings of each cited verse. Finally, he adopted two principles of Quranic interpretation: 'Every general remains general until something specifies it' and 'The lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the particularity of the occasion.' These principles are crucial in deriving educational lessons from Quranic verses without being limited to the specific circumstances of revelation.

Key Terms
القائمة المحكمة
المصادر التفسيرية
كل عام يبقى على عمومه حتى يأتي ما يخصصه
العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the two principles adopted by the author primarily ensure?
A) That only the literal meaning of the text is considered.
B) That the generality of the wording is preserved unless a specifier exists.
C) That the occasion of revelation always limits the ruling.
D) That exegetical sources are not needed.

Key Takeaway
The principles of 'general remains general' and 'lesson from wording, not occasion' are essential for deriving universal rulings from the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the principle 'Every general remains general until specified,' determine whether the verse 'And do not kill yourselves' (4:29) applies to all forms of self-harm, and identify any specifying texts that might limit its application.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Role of Tafsir in Educational Inference
Author details his research methodology for collecting and verifying Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author concluded that returning to tafsir sources was inevitable for extracting educational implications from Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Tafsir sources provide the authoritative meaning needed for directed educational inference.

After receiving varied feedback from the expert committee, the author realized that his initial list alone was insufficient. The consensus among scholars was that well-known tafsir works must be consulted to understand verses that do not carry obvious educational implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the limitation of the list
The author's initial list of verses, though carefully compiled, did not provide the detailed interpretation needed for educational inference.
The list included citation accuracy but not full exegetical context.
2
Understand the expert consensus
The arbitrators agreed on the necessity of returning to well-known tafsir sources.
They emphasized the researcher's need to skillfully handle tafsir texts.
3
Contrast with initial approach
Initially, the author aimed to avoid relying on tafsir sources due to differences in purpose and style between ancient and modern works.
But the feedback convinced him that tafsir was unavoidable.
4
Adopt the interpretive principles
The author then adopted two usul al-fiqh principles to guide his use of tafsir, ensuring consistent interpretation.
These principles help in deriving educational lessons from verses that may not have explicit educational content.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Two Hermeneutical Principles for Interpreting the Quran
Author states two methodological rules for interpreting Quranic verses with examples from Tabari and Ibn Taymiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two hermeneutical principles (general remains general until specified; lesson from generality of wording, not specificity of cause) to interpret Quranic verses correctly.

Core Idea
The Quran's general words cover all cases unless evidence specifies, and a verse's ruling applies beyond its specific occasion.

This lesson introduces two foundational rules of Quranic interpretation from the uṣūl al-fiqh tradition. These rules protect against arbitrary restrictions and ensure consistent application of divine commands and prohibitions. The first rule presumes generality of terms; the second extends rulings from a specific cause to all similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify general terms in a verse
Look for words like 'كل' (all) or other general expressions. For example, in verse 2:116, the word 'كل' (all) encompasses every being in the heavens and earth.
2
Apply the first rule: assume generality
Presume that the general term covers all its individuals unless you find evidence (mukhaṣṣiṣ) that specifies it. Al-Ṭabarī rejected the claim that 'كل' refers only to obedient believers, demanding a proof for restriction.
This rule is a default principle in uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Recognize the occasion of revelation (sabab)
Identify the specific event that prompted the verse. For example, verse 2:116 was revealed in response to the claim that Allah has a son.
The occasion does not automatically limit the verse's application.
4
Apply the second rule: general wording over specific cause
Understand that the ruling of the verse applies not only to the original cause but to every similar case. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that no scholar restricts generalities to a specific individual; they apply to the type.
If the verse is a command or prohibition, it applies to others like the original person; if it is a report, it includes those in a similar position.
5
Practice with the provided example
Reconstruct al-Ṭabarī's argument: the word 'كل' is general; those who restricted it to obedient believers failed to provide valid evidence. The correct approach is to maintain generality until proof of specification arrives.
This demonstrates how the first rule works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- تم الاعتماد على قاعدتي: كل عام يبقى على عمومه حتى يأتي ما يخصصه، و((العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب.
- فالقاعدة الأولى استند إليها الطبري (ت: 310هـ) في تفسيره (جامع البيان) كمنهج مطّرد. وقال في تفسير قول الله تعالى - على سبيل المثال -: ﴿وَقالُوا اتَّخَذَ اللَّهُ وَلَداً سُبْحانَهُ بَلْ لَهُ ما فِي السَّماواتِ وَالْأَرْضِ كُلٌّ لَهُ قانِتُونَ﴾ [البقرة: 116/2]. ..... وقد زعم بعض من قصرت معرفته عن توجيه الكلام وجهته أن قوله: (كل) له (قانتون خاصة لأهل الطاعة وليست بعامة وغير جائز ادعاء خصوص في آية عام ظاهرها؛ إلا بحجة يجب التسليم لها ...)).
أما القاعدة الثانية فقد قال عنها ابن تيمية (ت: 728هـ): ((والناس وإن تنازعوا في اللفظ العام الوارد على سبب : هل يختص بسببه أو لا ؟ فلم يقل أحد علماء المسلمين: إنّ عمومات الكتاب والسنة تختص بالشخص المعين، وإنما من غاية ما يقال: إنها تختص بنوع ذلك الشخص، فيعم ما يشبهه، ولا يكون العموم فيها بحسب اللفظ، والآية التي لها سبب معين إن كانت أمراً أو نهياً فهي متناولة لذلك الشخص ولغيره ممن كان بمنزلته، وإن كانت خيراً بمدح أو ذم فهي متناولة لذلك الشخص ولغيره ممن كان منزلته).
- I relied on two rules: every general (ʿāmm) remains upon its generality until something comes to specify it, and 'the lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the specificity of the occasion.'
- As for the first rule, al-Ṭabarī (d. 310 AH) relied on it in his interpretation (Jāmiʿ al-Bayān) as a consistent method. He said in interpreting the saying of God Almighty – for example –: {They say, 'Allah has taken a son.' Exalted is He! Rather, to Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth; all are devoutly obedient to Him} [al-Baqarah 2:116]. ..... And some whose knowledge fell short of directing speech in its proper direction claimed that His saying 'all' are 'devoutly obedient' is specifically for the people of obedience and is not general; and it is not permissible to claim specificity in a verse that is general in its apparent meaning except with evidence that must be accepted...
As for the second rule, Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) said about it: 'People have disputed concerning the general wording that occurs upon a cause: is it restricted to its cause or not? Not a single scholar of the Muslims has said that the generalities of the Book and the Sunnah are restricted to a specific individual. At most it is said: they are restricted to the type of that person, so it includes what resembles him, and the generality in them is not according to the wording. The verse that has a specific cause: if it is a command or prohibition, it applies to that person and to others who are in his position; if it is a report praising or condemning, it applies to that person and to others who are in his position.'
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by the author, explaining the two fundamental rules he adopts for interpreting Quranic verses. The first rule is: 'every general remains general until something comes to specify it.' This is a standard uṣūlī principle that the default for a general term (ʿāmm) is to cover all its individuals unless there is evidence (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to restrict it. The author then gives an example from al-Ṭabarī, who applied this rule in his tafsīr. Al-Ṭabarī refutes the claim that the word 'all' in verse 2:116 refers only to obedient believers, insisting that the general wording must be taken as general unless a valid proof establishes specificity. The second rule is: 'the lesson is from the generality of the wording, not from the specificity of the cause.' This means that when a verse was revealed about a particular event or person, its ruling applies not just to that case but to every similar case. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that no Muslim scholar restricts Quranic and prophetic generalities to a specific individual; at most they restrict it to the type of that person, so that it includes others like him. The author then notes that these two rules are consistently applied throughout his study.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm)
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ)
العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب (al-ʿibra bi-ʿumūm al-lafẓ lā bi-khuṣūṣ al-sabab)
سبب (sabab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, what does Ibn Taymiyya say about a general wording that occurs upon a specific cause?
A) It is restricted to that specific cause only.
B) It is not restricted to the specific individual but applies to others like him.
C) It is ambiguous and requires further evidence for any application.
D) It applies only to the person mentioned and no one else.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's general words are presumed general, and its rulings extend beyond the specific occasion of revelation to all similar cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a Quranic verse that was revealed about a specific event (e.g., 33:37 about Zayd's divorce) and explain how the second principle ensures its ruling applies to all similar situations.
?
Find a verse with a general word (e.g., 'al-sāriq' in 5:38) and identify what evidence could specify it.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The First Interpretive Rule: General Verses Remain General
First rule: general Quranic verses remain general despite specific causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a general Quranic verse retains its generality despite having a specific cause of revelation.

Core Idea
A specific cause does not restrict a general divine command.

In interpreting the Quran, it is crucial to know when a verse's application is limited to its historical context or remains universal. This lesson presents the first rule from the author's methodology, based on al-Tabari's tafsir, which establishes that general wording is not curtailed by the occasion of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule's core claim
The rule states that a verse with a general meaning (like 'كل له قانتون') is not made specific by the event that occasioned its revelation. Al-Tabari relied on this as a consistent methodology.
Even if a verse was revealed about a specific person or group, its general wording remains applicable to all.
2
Examine al-Tabari's example
Consider verse 2:116: 'All are devoutly obedient to Him.' Some claimed 'All' refers only to obedient believers, but al-Tabari rejected this because the wording is general and there is no evidence to restrict it.
The claim of specification must be supported by a compelling proof (hujja).
3
Understand the consequence
Unless there is clear evidence to restrict the general meaning, it applies universally. This rule upholds the broad applicability of Quranic statements.
This prevents arbitrary limitations based on historical circumstances.
4
Connect to usul al-fiqh
This rule is a foundational principle in Islamic legal theory: general verses (عمومات الكتاب) retain their generality even if they have a specific sabab al-nuzul.
The rule safeguards the universal guidance of the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة
- فالقاعدة الأولى استند إليها الطبري (ت: ۳۱۰هـ) في تفسيره (جامع البيان) كمنهج مطّرد. وقال في تفسير قول الله تعالى - على سبيل المثال -: (وَقالُوا اتَّخَذَ اللَّهُ وَلَداً سُبْحانَهُ بَلْ لَهُ ما فِي السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ كُلٌّ لَهُ قانتون) [البقرة: ١١٦/٢]. وقد زعم بعض من قصرت معرفته عن توجيه الكلام وجهته أن قوله: (كل) له (قانتون) خاصة لأهل الطاعة وليست بعامة وغير جائز ادعاء خصوص في آية عام ظاهرها؛ إلا بحجة يجب التسليم لها ...
أما القاعدة الثانية فقد قال عنها ابن تيمية (ت: ۷۲۸ هـ): "والناس وإن تنازعوا في اللفظ العام الوارد على سبب : هل يختص بسببه أو لا ؟ فلم يقل أحد علماء المسلمين: إنّ عمومات الكتاب والسنة تختص بالشخص المعين، وإنما من غاية ما يقال: إنها تختص بنوع ذلك الشخص، فيعم ما يشبهه، ولا يكون العموم فيها بحسب اللفظ، والآية التي لها سبب معين إن كانت أمراً أو نهياً فهي متناولة لذلك الشخص ولغيره ممن كان بمنزلته، وإن كانت خيراً بمدح أو ذم فهي متناولة لذلك الشخص ولغيره ممن كان منزلته."
أقول : وقد تجسّد الالتزام بهاتين القاعدتين عبر أبواب هذه الدراسة، ولعله أكثر وضوحاً في فصل أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها في القرآن الكريم.
ومن الإجراءات التقليدية تم ما يلي:
- عزو الآيات جميعها إلى مظانها في المصحف الشريف.
- تخريج الأحاديث على الرغم من محدوديتها الكمية نظراً لطبيعة الدراسة المتمحورة حول القرآن الكريم.
- الرجوع المباشر إلى المراجع الأصلية التي أفاد منها الباحث في دراسته، ولا تكاد توجد إحالة إلى مرجع ثانوي إلا في حالات يتعذر معها الوصول إلى المرجع الأصلي، ولندرة هذه الحالات فلا تكاد تذكر.
- عمدت إلى استخراج واستنباط المضامين التربوية المباشرة التي تضمنتها نظرية المعرفة في القرآن الكريم، وإلى إثبات ذلك في سياقه الطبيعي عبر مباحث هذه الدراسة خاصة إذا ما تعلق التضمين بالمنهج أو أحد عناصره أو تعلق بالمعلم أو بالمتعلم، أو بفلسفة التربية ومرتكزاتها. وقد كان هذا الاستقرار على هذا الأسلوب بعد محاولات متعدّدة كي تكون مخصصة بباب أو بفصل مستقل، أو تلحق بآخر كل باب أو فصل، ولكن رأيت الرابط بينها غير واضح لمن سيقرأ تلك التضمينات منفصلة عن النظرية، علاوة على أنه يتعذر فصل جوانب تربوية في أصل النظرية، ثم إن الاستئناس بطريقة الذين كتبوا في فلسفة التربية ومنهم من يعدّ من روادها كفيليب فينكس - مثلاً - الذي نحي المنحى ذاته المطبق هاهنا، ذلك أيضا مما دفع لالتزام هذا المسلك وتحبيذه على ما سواه.
- اصطلحت على أن أعتبر أسم المؤلف، ثم عنوان الكتاب ثم رقم الجزء - إن وجد - أو الصفحة كافياً للدلالة على أهم ما يعني القارئ في هامش الرسالة، أما بقية معلومات النشر فمثبتة بكاملها في قائمة المراجع في آخر الدراسة. ويلاحظ أنه يكتب عنوان الكتاب كاملاً لأول مرة يرد فيها، إلا أن يزيد على كلمتين أو ثلاثا في حال الإضافة أو الوصف وما في حكمهما ككتاب فلسفة التربية للحال الأولى، وكتاب فلسفة التربية الإسلامية للحال الثانية، أما إذا زاد عن ذلك فأكتفي بذكر المؤلف فقط، مع الإشارة إلى كونه مرجعًا سابقا، أما إن كان للمؤلف أكثر من كتاب أو دراسة مستخدمة في هذه الدراسة فأضطر إلى ذكر جزء من العنوان بما يحقق غرض التمييز بينه وبين العناوين الأخرى للمؤلف. ولا يستثنى من ذلك إلا المراجع الأجنبية نظراً لمحدوديتها.
Introduction. - The first rule was relied upon by al-Tabari (d. 310 AH) in his exegesis (Jami' al-Bayan) as a consistent methodology. He said, interpreting the saying of Allah Almighty - for example -: 'And they say, 'Allah has taken a son.' Exalted is He! Rather, to Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth. All are devoutly obedient to Him.' [al-Baqarah 2:116]. Some whose knowledge fell short of directing speech to its proper course claimed that His saying: 'All' to Him 'are obedient' is specific to the people of obedience and not general, and it is not permissible to claim specification in a verse whose apparent meaning is general, except with evidence that must be submitted to... As for the second rule, Ibn Taymiyyah (d. 728 AH) said about it: 'People, though they dispute over a general word that occurs due to a cause: is it specific to its cause or not? Not one of the Muslim scholars has said that the general statements of the Book and the Sunnah are specific to the specific person. The utmost that is said is that they are specific to the type of that person, so they include what resembles him. The generality in them is not according to the wording. A verse that has a specific cause – if it is a command or prohibition – it is applicable to that person and to others who are in his position. If it is good news of praise or blame, it is applicable to that person and to others who are in his position.' I say: Commitment to these two rules has been embodied throughout the chapters of this study, and perhaps it is most evident in the chapter on the ethics of knowledge and its unity in the Holy Quran. And among the traditional procedures taken are the following: - Attributing all verses to their locations in the Mushaf. - Authenticating hadiths despite their limited quantity due to the nature of the study centered on the Holy Quran. - Direct reference to original sources utilized by the researcher in his study; there are hardly any references to secondary sources except in cases where access to the original source is impossible, and due to the rarity of these cases, they are hardly worth mentioning. - I set out to extract and deduce the direct educational implications contained in the theory of knowledge in the Holy Quran, and to establish that in its natural context through the investigations of this study, especially if the implication relates to the methodology or any of its elements, or to the teacher or learner, or to the philosophy of education and its foundations. This stability on this method came after multiple attempts to make it specific to a chapter or an independent section, or attached to the end of each chapter or section, but I saw that the connection between them would not be clear for one who reads those implications separated from the theory, in addition to the impossibility of separating educational aspects that are inherent in the theory itself. Moreover, following the method of those who wrote in the philosophy of education, including some considered pioneers like Philip Phenix - for example - who took the same approach applied here, that also pushed me to adhere to this path and prefer it over others. - I have adopted a convention to consider the author's name, then the book title, then the volume number – if any – or page number as sufficient to indicate the most important thing that concerns the reader in the margin of the thesis. As for the rest of the publication information, it is fully listed in the bibliography at the end of the study. It is noted that the full book title is written the first time it appears, unless it exceeds two or three words when it includes an addition or description and the like, such as 'Philosophy of Education' in the first case, and 'Islamic Philosophy of Education' in the second. If it exceeds that, I suffice with mentioning the author only, with a note that it is a previous reference. But if the author has more than one book or study used in this study, I am forced to mention part of the title to achieve the purpose of distinguishing it from the author's other titles. Foreign references are exempted from this due to their limited number.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an introduction where the author outlines the methodological foundations of the study. The author begins by presenting two key interpretive rules that underpin the approach. The first rule, derived from al-Tabari, asserts that a Quranic verse whose wording is general remains general in application even if it was revealed due to a specific cause. The author provides an example: the verse 'All are devoutly obedient to Him' (2:116) is general, and some wrongly claimed it was specific to obedient people. The second rule, from Ibn Taymiyya, clarifies that general statements in the Quran and Sunnah are not limited to the specific person who occasioned them but extend to all similar persons or cases. This rule applies to commands, prohibitions, and statements of praise or blame. The author then notes that these two rules are consistently applied throughout the study, especially in the chapter on the ethics of knowledge. Following this, the author lists several traditional research procedures: citing all verses to their Quranic locations, verifying hadiths, using original sources directly, deriving educational implications within their natural context rather than in separate sections, and adopting a concise citation method for references. The author justifies embedding educational implications within the theoretical discussion rather than isolating them, explaining that they are integral to the theory and better understood in context.

القاعدة الأولى
سبب النزول
عمومات الكتاب

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Second Interpretive Rule: Texts Apply to the Type, Not the Individual
Second rule: general texts apply to the type of the person, not the individual exclusively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate Ibn Taymiyya's rule that general texts apply to the type of the person, not the individual exclusively.

Core Idea
Quranic commands and prohibitions address types, not individuals.

The second rule, articulated by Ibn Taymiyya, clarifies that general verses and hadiths are not limited to the specific person who occasioned them but apply to anyone of the same type. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Scholars disagree whether a general word issued due to a cause pertains only to that cause or remains general. Ibn Taymiyya states that the utmost claim is that it pertains to the type (naw') of that person, not the individual.
This is a middle position: not absolute generality, but not individual specificity either.
2
Apply to commands and prohibitions
If a verse commands or prohibits something to a specific person, it covers that person and anyone in a similar position (man kāna bi-manzilatih).
For example, the command to the Prophet to 'seek forgiveness' applies to all believers in similar positions.
3
Apply to praise or blame
If a verse praises or blames a specific person, it applies to that person and others who share his position or characteristics.
This ensures that divine praise/criticism is not tied to historical figures alone.
4
Distinguish from the first rule
The first rule maintains generality of wording; the second rule clarifies that the generality extends to the type, not every individual indiscriminately. It recognizes that the cause may indicate a subgroup.
Ibn Taymiyya says 'wa lā yakūn al-‘umūm fīhā biḥasab al-lafẓ' (generality is not according to the wording), meaning it is not literal generality but generality by type.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an introduction where the author outlines the methodological foundations of the study. The author begins by presenting two key interpretive rules that underpin the approach. The first rule, derived from al-Tabari, asserts that a Quranic verse whose wording is general remains general in application even if it was revealed due to a specific cause. The author provides an example: the verse 'All are devoutly obedient to Him' (2:116) is general, and some wrongly claimed it was specific to obedient people. The second rule, from Ibn Taymiyya, clarifies that general statements in the Quran and Sunnah are not limited to the specific person who occasioned them but extend to all similar persons or cases. This rule applies to commands, prohibitions, and statements of praise or blame. The author then notes that these two rules are consistently applied throughout the study, especially in the chapter on the ethics of knowledge. Following this, the author lists several traditional research procedures: citing all verses to their Quranic locations, verifying hadiths, using original sources directly, deriving educational implications within their natural context rather than in separate sections, and adopting a concise citation method for references. The author justifies embedding educational implications within the theoretical discussion rather than isolating them, explaining that they are integral to the theory and better understood in context.

عمومات الكتاب والسنة

In academic research, defining the scope is critical to avoid ambiguity. This passage from a study on Quranic epistemology explicitly states what it includes and excludes. By parsing these boundaries, you learn how to set proper limits in Islamic research.

Core Idea
A thorough literature review first acknowledges what is NOT there.

This passage shows how a researcher in Islamic epistemology first admits that no study matches his exact topic, then presents three partially related works. This approach teaches us to be honest about the boundaries of existing literature and to classify studies by their relevance. We will learn to parse such a review and assess the scope of each study.

Three Studies on Quranic Epistemology
Author overviews three studies on Quranic epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract and compare the main epistemological themes from three academic studies on Quranic epistemology.

Core Idea
Three studies compare Quranic and philosophical epistemology, highlighting the Quran's unique stance on reason and revelation.

Academic studies often survey multiple sources to map out the landscape of knowledge in Islam. This passage summarizes three such studies, each approaching Quranic epistemology from a different angle. By learning to parse these summaries, you will sharpen your ability to identify key epistemological claims and methods used in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify each study's scope and structure
Look at the first study: it is divided into three 'books' covering theory, nature, and origin/types of knowledge. The second study is a thesis on sources of knowledge, with four chapters. The third study focuses on rational contemplation ('al-nazar al-'aqli') in the Quran.
Each study uses a comparative method (al-manhaj al-muqarin) to contrast Quranic and philosophical views.
2
Extract the main epistemological conclusion from each study
The first study recommends directing theological and philosophical studies toward Quranic texts. The second concludes that philosophical thought fails without divine guidance. The third emphasizes the Quran's explicit call to use reason and notes that 'knowledge' appears over 900 times.
These conclusions show a spectrum from prioritizing revelation to affirming reason within a Quranic framework.
3
Compare the methods used across the studies
All three employ the comparative method, contrasting Quranic teachings with philosophical schools. The first study uses a three-book structure, the second a thematic-chapter approach, and the third a four-chapter layout focusing on rational contemplation.
The comparative method is a key tool in Islamic epistemology for distinguishing Quranic from non-Quranic sources.
4
Identify the epistemological sources discussed
The studies mention sensory, rational, intuitive, prophetic, and revealed knowledge. The second study explicitly lists revelation, inspiration, sense, reason, and sensory experience. This set corresponds to the classical Islamic categories of knowledge sources.
Understanding these categories is fundamental to further study in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

In light of this result, the researcher recommended calling on specialists in the field of philosophy to adopt the source of divine revelation in the field of the source of knowledge in order to overcome the stage of confusion and intellectual disturbance occurring so far.

The researcher concluded her study with a comprehensive summary of the research results, most notably that the call of the Holy Quran to use and employ the mind is an explicit call that does not accept interpretation. Moreover, the Quran also paid attention to the fruit of rational contemplation, which is knowledge and cognition, as the word 'knowledge' appears in the Quran more than 900 times. As for the relationship between the two sources of reason and revelation, it is eternal since God created Adam, the father of humanity.

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview of three academic studies on Quranic epistemology. The author first describes the first study (the book's own structure?), which is divided into three books: the first compares Quranic and philosophical epistemology, the second examines the nature of knowledge comparatively, and the third explores the origin, methods, and types of knowledge (sensory, rational, intuitive, prophetic). Recommendations are given to direct studies toward Quranic texts. Then the second study by Dr. al-Zanidi is summarized: it covers sources of knowledge (revelation, inspiration, sense, reason, sensory experience) and concludes that philosophical thought fails without divine guidance. Finally, the third study by Fatima Ismail is outlined: it focuses on rational contemplation in the Quran, emphasizing the explicit call to use reason and the frequent mention of 'knowledge' in the Quran. The passage thus serves as a literature review framing the author's own work.

The Limits of Reason and the Need for Revelation
Summary of conclusions from three studies on Quran and reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological stance that reason alone is insufficient and requires revelation, as articulated in the passage's critique of philosophical thought.

Core Idea
Reason without revelation leads to confusion; true knowledge requires integrating both.

This passage presents the conclusions of three modern studies on the relationship between the Quran and rational thought. The author argues that philosophical inquiry relying solely on human reason has failed to achieve happiness and recommends adopting divine revelation as a source of knowledge. This lesson helps you unpack that argument and see how it connects to classical Islamic debates on epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion of the first study
The researcher concluded that philosophical thought still seeks fixed rules for happiness but has failed because it relied solely on human reason and distanced itself from God's guidance.
2
Recognize the problem with exclusive reliance on reason
The passage states that reason alone was not equipped to reach truth in certain areas, leading to confusion and intellectual disorder.
3
Note the recommendation to adopt revelation
The researcher called on philosophers to adopt divine revelation as a source of knowledge (masdariyyat al-ma'rifa) to overcome confusion.
4
Compare with the third study's emphasis on integration
The third study highlights that the Quran calls explicitly for using reason, but also emphasizes that reason and revelation are complementary since creation, with revelation supporting reason.

واختتم الباحث دراسته بتقرير النتيجة الكلية التالية: إن الفكر في إطاره الفلسفي ما يزال يبحث عن قواعد ثابتة تحقق سعادة الإنسان، خصوصاً بعد حصوله على معرفة واسعة إن في مجال العلم التجريبي، أو في مجال معرفة سنن الله في الكون والحياة فتحققت أهدافه المادية ونجحت إلى حد كبير، بيد أنه في المجال الفلسفي لم يحقق ذلك نتيجة ابتعاده عن هدى الله، حينما ركن إلى العقل البشري وحده في مجالات لم يزود بما يؤهله لبلوغ الحقيقة فيها. وفي ضوء هذه النتيجة أوصى الباحث بدعوة المتخصصين في مجال الفلسفة إلى تبني مصدرية الوحي الإلهي في مجال مصدرية المعرفة كي يتجاوزوا مرحلة الحيرة والاضطراب الفكري الحاصل حتى الآن. • الدراسة الثالثة (القرآن والنظر العقلي) للباحثة فاطمة إسماعيل محمد إسماعيل، والدراسة رسالة ماجستير تقدمت بها الباحثة إلى قسم الفلسفة لنيل درجة الماجستير. وتكونت الرسالة من 351 صفحة من القطع الكبير. قام بنشرها المعهد العالمي للفكر الإسلامي بأمريكا، ضمن رسائل المعهد كذلك. تكونت الدراسة من أربعة فصول مع تمهيد وخاتمة، شملت: القرآن بين النظر العقلي وطلب العلم والمعرفة ومنهج النظر العقلي في القرآن، ودور النظر العقلي في بناء عقيدة المسلم وأثر النظر العقلي في القرآن على الفكر الإسلامي. واختتمت الباحثة دراستها بخلاصة جامعة لنتائج البحث وأبرزها أن دعوة القرآن الكريم لاستخدام العقل وتوظيفه دعوة صريحة لا تقبل التأويل. ثم إن القرآن أيضًا اهتم بثمرة النظر العقلي، وهو العلم والمعرفة، حيث ورد لفظ العلم في القرآن أكثر من 900 مرّة. أما العلاقة بين مصدري العقل والنقل فأزلية منذ أن خلق الله آدم أبا البشرية. ويلاحظ أن الدراسة الأولى والثانية قد التقتا في نظرية المعرفة وفق المنهج المقارن، وإن كانت الأولى قد اقتصرت على القرآن الكريم، في حين شملت الأخرى القرآن والسنة، مع أن النص القرآني ظل مهيمنا على محمل الدراسة. كما يلاحظ تغطية الدراسة الأولى لجميع عناصر النظرية في حين اقتصرت الأخرى على المصادر. لقد أفدت من الدراستين كلتيهما في الإسهام لبلورة وتشكيل الإطار العام للدراسة، مع مراعاة الفارق في المنهجية بين هذه الدراسة، وتينك الدراستين. أما الدراسة الثالثة فقد انفردت عن الدراستين الأوليين باقتصارها على جزئية واحدة من مصادر المعرفة وطرائقها، وهي القرآن الكريم في نظرته إلى العقل بما أثبت تكامل الوحي مع العقل في هذه المصدرية. ومن هذا المنطلق فقد مضيت في وضع مسلّمة هذه الدراسة، بحسبان الخلود والإطلاق للنص القرآني لا يتحققان إلا بمؤازرة الوحي للعقل ليستوعب الماضي والحاضر ويتجاوز بهما إلى المستقبل.
The researcher concluded his study with the following overall result: That thought within its philosophical framework still seeks fixed rules that achieve human happiness, especially after obtaining extensive knowledge, whether in the field of empirical science or in the field of knowing the laws of God in the universe and life, so its material goals have been achieved and succeeded to a large extent. However, in the philosophical field, it did not achieve that as a result of its distance from God's guidance, when it relied solely on human reason in areas for which it was not equipped to reach the truth. In light of this result, the researcher recommended calling on specialists in the field of philosophy to adopt the source of divine revelation in the field of the source of knowledge in order to overcome the stage of confusion and intellectual disorder that has occurred until now. • The third study (The Quran and Rational Inquiry) by researcher Fatima Ismail Muhammad Ismail, and the study is a master's thesis submitted by the researcher to the Department of Philosophy for the master's degree. The thesis consisted of 351 large pages. It was published by the International Institute of Islamic Thought in America, as part of the institute's theses as well. The study consisted of four chapters with an introduction and conclusion, including: The Quran between rational inquiry and the pursuit of knowledge and science, the method of rational inquiry in the Quran, the role of rational inquiry in building the Muslim's creed, and the impact of rational inquiry in the Quran on Islamic thought. The researcher concluded her study with a comprehensive summary of the research findings, most notably that the call of the Holy Quran to use and employ reason is an explicit call that does not allow interpretation. Furthermore, the Quran also cared about the fruit of rational inquiry, which is knowledge and science, as the word 'knowledge' (al-ilm) appears in the Quran more than 900 times. As for the relationship between the two sources of reason and revelation, it is eternal since God created Adam, the father of humanity. It is noted that the first and second studies met in the theory of knowledge according to the comparative method, although the first was limited to the Holy Quran, while the other included the Quran and Sunnah, with the Quranic text remaining dominant in both studies. It is also noted that the first study covered all elements of the theory while the other was limited to sources. I benefited from both studies in contributing to the crystallization and formation of the general framework of the study, taking into account the difference in methodology between this study and those two studies. As for the third study, it stood out from the first two studies by limiting itself to one part of the sources and methods of knowledge, namely the Holy Quran in its view of reason, as it established the integration of revelation with reason in this source. From this standpoint, I proceeded to set the premise of this study, considering that the eternity and absoluteness of the Quranic text are only achieved through the support of revelation for reason to comprehend the past and present and transcend them to the future.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an introduction in which the author summarizes three previous studies and explains how they inform the current work. The first part presents the conclusion of the first study: philosophical thought still seeks fixed rules for happiness, but relying solely on reason has failed because it distanced itself from divine guidance. The researcher recommended that philosophers adopt revelation as a source of knowledge. The passage then describes the third study, a master's thesis by Fatima Ismail, which has 351 pages and examines the Quran's view on rational inquiry. It covers topics like the relationship between Quran and reason, methodology, and its impact on Islamic thought. The key finding is that the Quran explicitly calls for using reason, and the word 'knowledge' appears over 900 times. The author notes differences and similarities among the studies: the first two share a comparative epistemology but differ in scope; the third focuses solely on the Quran's perspective. Finally, the author states that this study's premise is that the Quran's eternity is realized only when revelation supports reason to comprehend past, present, and future.

Comparative Epistemology: Revelation and Intellect
Author outlines comparative methodology and integration of revelation and intellect in three studies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the comparative methodology and the integration of revelation and intellect as foundational epistemological principles in the author's framework.

Core Idea
Revelation and intellect are not rivals; their integration is key to comprehending the eternal Quranic text.

This passage introduces the author's epistemological approach across three studies. You will learn how the author compares different scopes of inquiry and establishes the complementary relationship between divine revelation and human reason. This lesson will equip you to recognize the comparative method and the principle of integration as they appear in Islamic epistemological works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three studies and their scopes
Read the passage and note how the first study focuses solely on the Quran, the second includes both Quran and Sunnah, and the third narrows to the Quran's view on the intellect. Observe the comparative dimension.
The first study covers all elements of theory; the second only sources.
2
Extract the comparative methodology
The author uses a comparative approach (al-manhaj al-muqaran) to contrast the studies. Look for phrases indicating convergence, limitation, and distinction.
3
Locate the thesis on revelation and intellect
Find where the author states that the Quran affirms the integration (takamul) of revelation with the intellect as sources of knowledge. This is the core epistemological claim.
4
Understand the premise about the Quranic text
The author concludes that the eternity and absoluteness of the Quranic text require revelation's support for the intellect. Identify this as the foundational premise for the study.
The intellect alone cannot encompass past, present, and future without revelation.

ويلاحظ أن الدراسة الأولى والثانية قد التقتا في نظرية المعرفة وفق المنهج المقارن، وإن كانت الأولى قد اقتصرت على القرآن الكريم، في حين شملت الأخرى القرآن والسنة، مع أن النص القرآني ظل مهيمنا على محمل الدراسة. كما يلاحظ تغطية الدراسة الأولى لجميع عناصر النظرية في حين اقتصرت الأخرى على المصادر.
