للدراسة، مع مرا أما الدراسة الثالثة فقد انفردت عن الدراستين الأوليين باقتصارها على جزئية واحدة من مصادر المعرفة وطرائقها، وهي القرآن الكريم في نظرته إلى العقل بما أثبت تكامل الوحي مع العقل في هذه المصدرية. ومن هذا المنطلق فقد مضيت في وضع مسلّمة هذه الدراسة، بحسبان الخلود والإطلاق للنص القرآني لا يتحققان إلا بمؤازرة الوحي للعقل ليستوعب الماضي والحاضر ويتجاوز بهما إلى المستقبل.
Introduction. It is noted that the first and second studies met in the theory of knowledge according to the comparative methodology, although the first was limited to the Noble Quran, while the other included the Quran and the Sunnah, with the Quranic text remaining dominant in the subject of the study. It is also noted that the first study covered all elements of the theory, while the other was limited to the sources. I have benefited from both studies in contributing to the crystallization and formation of the general framework, taking into account the difference in methodology between this study and those two studies. For the study, with regard to... As for the third study, it distinguished itself from the first two by limiting itself to one particular aspect of the sources and methods of knowledge, namely the Noble Quran in its view of the intellect, affirming the integration of revelation with the intellect in this sourcehood. From this standpoint, I proceeded to set the premise of this study, considering that the eternity and absoluteness of the Quranic text are only achieved through the support of revelation for the intellect to encompass the past and present and transcend them to the future.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a preface explaining the methodological foundation of the work. The author begins by noting that the first two studies converge in the theory of knowledge using a comparative approach, but the first focuses solely on the Quran while the second includes both Quran and Sunnah, yet the Quranic text remains central. The first study comprehensively covers all components of the theory, whereas the second restricts itself to the sources. The author acknowledges benefiting from both to shape the general framework while noting methodological differences. Then, the third study is introduced as distinct because it narrows its focus to one specific element of knowledge sources and methods: the Quran's perspective on the intellect. The key thesis is that the Quran demonstrates the integration of revelation and intellect as sources of knowledge. The author then states the premise: the eternity and absoluteness of the Quranic text are only realized through revelation's support of the intellect, enabling it to comprehend past, present, and future. This establishes the epistemological stance of the work, emphasizing the complementary relationship between divine revelation and human reason.

Navigating the Introduction to Theory of Knowledge
Book presents chapter and section headings for theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main sections of the introduction to the theory of knowledge and explain their purpose.

Core Idea
A clear map of the chapter structure prevents confusion when diving into deep topics.

Before we dive into the content of the theory of knowledge, it is crucial to understand how the author has organized this introduction. The passage provides the chapter and section headings, which serve as a roadmap. This lesson will help you orient yourself to the structure so that you can follow the arguments more easily.

Guided Path
1
Locate the chapter title
Find the Arabic phrase 'الباب الأول' and 'نظرية المعرفة'. What is the overall theme of this chapter?
The phrase 'نظرية المعرفة' means 'theory of knowledge'.
2
Identify the two sections
The passage lists two sections: الفصل الأول (First Section) and الفصل الثاني (Second Section). What are their titles?
They are 'تطور نظرية المعرفة' (Development) and 'التعريفات' (Definitions).
3
Note the order
The development of the theory of knowledge comes before definitions. Why might the author order them this way?
Think about how understanding the historical development helps contextualize the definitions.
4
Connect to your learning
This structure tells you that you will first learn how the theory of knowledge developed, then learn specific definitions. Be prepared for historical context before technical terms.

Parsing the Book's First Gate Structure
Book presents the first gate's chapter titles on epistemology and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main topics of the first gate and explain why the author begins with epistemology.

Core Idea
The structure of this book reveals that definitions depend on epistemological foundations.

This lesson examines the opening structural marker of an Islamic scholarly work. The author begins with a gate (باب) on the theory of knowledge (نظرية المعرفة) before moving to definitions. Understanding why epistemology comes first is crucial for appreciating the author's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the gate and its title
Look at the Arabic heading: الباب الأول. This is the first major division. Note that it is called 'مدخل إلى نظرية المعرفة' (Introduction to the Theory of Knowledge).
In Islamic scholarly works, 'باب' is a major section.
2
List the chapters within the gate
Two chapters are listed: الفصل الأول: تطوّر نظرية المعرفة (The Development of the Theory of Knowledge) and الفصل الثاني: التعريفات (Definitions).
فصل means chapter or subsection.
3
Analyze the ordering
The author places theory of knowledge before definitions. Reflect on why: one must understand what knowledge is before defining things within that framework.
This reflects a common priority in Islamic epistemology: definitions are built on a prior theory of knowledge.
4
Connect to the broader work
Recognize that this is only the first gate of many. The author signals that the entire work is methodologically grounded in epistemological considerations.

This passage is a heading and table of contents for the first major division (باب) of the book. It announces that the first gate is an introduction (مدخل) to the theory of knowledge (نظرية المعرفة). It then lists two chapters: the first on the development of epistemology, and the second on definitions. Such structural markers are common in Arabic scholarly works to organize the material. The student should note that the work begins with epistemological foundations before proceeding to definitions, which indicates the author's methodological priority.

The Birth of Modern Epistemology with John Locke
Author narrates the historical emergence of modern epistemology with John Locke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical origin of modern Western epistemology and contrast it with the treatment of knowledge in the Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Modern epistemology as a discipline began with Locke, but Islamic scholars already deeply explored theory of knowledge.

This passage narrates the common scholarly view that epistemology in its modern sense was launched by John Locke in 1690. Understanding this origin helps us appreciate the distinct yet parallel developments in Islamic thought, where questions of knowledge (ʿilm) have always been central in fields like uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām. As you read, consider how Locke’s inquiry mirrors classical Islamic discussions on the sources and limits of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical claim
Notice what the passage asserts: that epistemology as we know it today appeared with John Locke in 1690. This is a specific claim about when the discipline became self-aware.
2
Understand the motivating problem
What prompted Locke? Unresolved discussions with friends on ethics and religion. He saw the need to examine the foundations – which questions are accessible to human reason and which are not.
3
Contrast with Islamic epistemology
In the Islamic tradition, the theory of knowledge (ʿilm) is foundational. For instance, al-Ghazālī and Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī wrote extensively on the classification of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (iktisābī). How does Locke's approach compare?
Reflect on whether the problems are similar or different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نشأة النظرية
الفصل الأول
تطوّر نظرية المعرفة
يذهب كثير من الباحثين إلى أن نظرية المعرفة لم تظهر في صورتها الراهنة والتي باتت تبحث في طبيعة المعرفة، ومصادرها وطرائقها، وإمكان الحصول عليها أو حدودها، إلا على يد الفيلسوف الإنجليزي جون لوك (ت: ١٧٠٤م) وذلك عام (١٦٩٠م). وقصة ذلك أنه على إثر مناقشات دارت بينه وبعض أصدقائه شتاء (١٦٧٠ / ١٦٧١م) لم يصلوا فيها إلى حل للمسائل التي أثاروها؛ فطن لوك إلى ضرورة فحص مبادئ الأخلاق والدين، والنظر في تلك القضايا أيها يكون في متناولنا؟ وأيها يفوق إدراكنا؟ فتكونت لديه فكرة البحث في المعرفة وكتب من فوره رسالة في العقل الإنساني عزا فيها كل المعاني إلى التجربة وبعد مرور ١٩ سنة (١٦٩٠م) كتب كتابه الشهير: (مقالة في الفهم الإنساني) والكتاب مجزأ إلى أربع مقالات، أسماها لوك كتباً تناول فيها ما يلي:
1 - الكتاب الأول: وعنوانه الرد على نظرية المعاني الغريزية في المعرفة بالوراثة.
The emergence of the theory
Chapter One
The development of epistemology
Many researchers hold that epistemology did not appear in its current form, which has come to investigate the nature, sources, methods, and possibility or limits of knowledge, except by the hand of the English philosopher John Locke (d. 1704 CE) in the year 1690 CE. The story is that following discussions that took place between him and some of his friends in the winter of 1670/1671 CE, in which they did not reach a solution to the issues they raised, Locke realized the necessity of examining the principles of ethics and religion, and considering which of those issues are within our reach and which exceed our comprehension. Thus the idea of researching knowledge occurred to him, and he immediately wrote a treatise on the human intellect in which he attributed all meanings to experience. After 19 years (1690 CE) he wrote his famous book: 'An Essay Concerning Human Understanding', which is divided into four essays, which Locke called books, covering the following:
1 - Book One: Its title is the refutation of the theory of innate ideas in knowledge by inheritance.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction to the development of epistemology, focusing on the modern period. The author begins by stating a common scholarly view: that epistemology in its contemporary sense—as a discipline investigating the nature, sources, methods, and limits of knowledge—was first established by the English philosopher John Locke in 1690. The author then recounts the anecdote that prompted Locke's inquiry: after fruitless discussions with friends in 1670-1671, Locke realized the need to examine the foundations of ethics and religion and to determine which questions are accessible to human reason and which are not. This led Locke to write a preliminary treatise attributing all concepts to experience, and later, in 1690, his magnum opus 'An Essay Concerning Human Understanding'. The author notes that the work is divided into four books, the first of which refutes the theory of innate ideas. This historical framing provides context for the subsequent discussion of Quranic epistemology. In the Islamic tradition, questions of knowledge (ʿilm) are central to uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām; for example, the classification of knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (iktisābī) mirrors the concerns Locke raises about the sources and limits of human understanding.

Muslim Precursors to Locke in Epistemology
Author argues Muslim scholars preceded Locke in systematic epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify at least three Muslim scholars who systematically discussed epistemology before John Locke and state their key contributions as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Muslim scholars systematically studied epistemology centuries before Locke.

This lesson examines a historical argument that early Muslim scholars laid the foundations of systematic epistemology long before John Locke. The author presents a list of scholars and their works to demonstrate their priority, urging us to recognize the cumulative nature of knowledge. You will trace this argument and evaluate its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central claim
Read the opening sentence: 'The truth is that research into knowledge and its elements is not an invention of Locke at all.' This is the thesis. Note that the author acknowledges Locke's systematic presentation but denies originality.
The author concedes Locke's technical organization but argues for earlier scattered contributions.
2
List the scholars and their works
Scan the passage for names and book titles: al-Baqillani (Tamhid al-Awa'il), al-Baghdadi (Usul al-Din), al-Razi (al-Mahsul), Ibn Taymiyya (Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql), al-Iji (al-Mawaqif). Write them down.
These works span the 5th to 8th centuries AH / 11th to 14th centuries CE.
3
Extract each scholar's epistemological contribution
For each scholar, note what specific epistemological topics they addressed. For example, al-Baqillani discussed the reality of knowledge, divided sciences into necessary and theoretical, and listed sources of knowledge. Do this for all five.
Ibn Taymiyya is highlighted for defining the paths of knowledge precisely and arguing for the unity of reason and revelation.
4
Assess the author's evidence
Consider whether the author provides sufficient evidence for each claim. Are the citations specific (with page references)? The author includes footnote numbers (2, 4, etc.) but does not quote the original texts. Evaluate whether the examples convincingly show systematic epistemology.
The argument relies on listing titles and topics; it does not quote definitions or arguments from the works.
5
Evaluate the conclusion about Locke
Read the final lines: 'So all this confirms that John Locke – it seems – benefited from those before him.' Reflect on whether the presented evidence supports that Locke actually borrowed, or merely that earlier work existed. The author uses 'it seems' (yabdu) which softens the claim.
This step engages with the logical strength of the historical inference.

والحق أن البحث في المعرفة وعناصرها ليس من إبداع (لوك) مطلقاً، وإن كان أهم ما تميز به طرحه للنظرية وفق هذا النسق المتسم بتنظيمه الفني وضبطه. أما مباحث النظرية بصورة متفرقة فإن الأمانة العلمية والإنصاف المنهجي يقضيان بالاعتراف لعلماء المسلمين الأوائل بالسبق إلى ذلك على نحو غير منكور. وعلى سبيل المثال فقد ألف أبو بكر الباقلاني (ت: ٤٠٣ هـ ) كتابه (تمهيد الأوائل وتلخيص الدلائل) وكان من أول مباحثه الكلام في حقيقة العلم ومعناه، وأقسام العلوم حيث قسمها إلى ضروري ونظري، ثم تناول مدارك العلوم أي طرق المعرفة (٢). كما تناول أبو منصور البغدادي (ت: ٤٢٩ هـ ) في كتابه (أصول الدين) الحقائق والعلوم، وعدها الأصل الأول من بين ١٥ أصلاً، فتناول حد العلم وحقيقته وإثبات العلم والحقائق، وأقسام العلوم، وأسماءها، والحواس وأقسامها، وإثبات العلوم النظرية، والخبر المتواتر طريقاً إلى العلم. وخصص فخر الدين الرازي (ت: ٦٠٦ هـ ) الفصل الرابع من بداية كتاب (المحصول في علم أصول الفقه) للبحث في تحديد معنى العلم والظن كما خصص الفصل الرابع للبحث في النظر والدليل والأمارة(٤). كما وتناول ابن تيمية (ت: ۷۲۸ هـ) أجزاء محددة من نظرية المعرفة في بعض أجزاء من كتاب (درء تعارض العقل والنقل) أو (موافقة صحيح المنقول لصريح المعقول). فقد عرض لمسائل العلم والنظر (۱) ، بل وحدد طرق المعرفة بصورة دقيقة وواضحة(٢). عرض هذا مع أن كتابه في جملته يتناول لب نظرية المعرفة حيث يناقش فرضية نشوب تعارض بين العقل والوحي، ويخلص إلى وحدة المعرفة في المصادر والطرائق. أما عضد الدين الإيجي (ت: ٧٥٦ هـ ) فقد جعل الموقف الأول من كتابه (المواقف) في علم الكلام خاصاً بالعلم والنظر، وفيه تناول تعريف العلم وأقسامه، والنظر، وما إذا كان يفيد العلم (٣). كما عاد فتكلم عن العلم وأقسامه، وأضداده من الجهل، وتحقيق ما إذا كانت إدراكات الحواس علماً أم لا؟(٤). إذن فكل ذلك يؤكد أن جون لوك - فيما يبدو – قد أفاد ممن سبق قليلاً أو كثيراً، وسواء أكان هؤلاء الذين أفاد منهم مسلمين أم غير مسلمين، فإن مبدأ وحدة المعرفة أو تراكميتها زاد معرفي لكل باحث.
The truth is that research into knowledge and its elements is not an invention of Locke at all, although the most important feature of his presentation of the theory is according to this format characterized by its technical organization and precision. As for the topics of the theory in a scattered manner, scientific honesty and methodological fairness require acknowledging the precedence of early Muslim scholars in that in an undeniable way. For example, Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) authored his book 'Tamhid al-Awa'il wa Talkhis al-Dala'il' and among its first topics was speech on the reality of knowledge and its meaning, and the divisions of sciences, where he divided them into necessary and theoretical, then he discussed the sources of sciences, i.e., the paths of knowledge (2). Likewise, Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH) in his book 'Usul al-Din' dealt with realities and sciences, counting them as the first principle among 15 principles. He discussed the definition of knowledge and its reality, proof of knowledge and realities, divisions of sciences, their names, the senses and their divisions, proof of theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a path to knowledge. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) devoted the fourth chapter at the beginning of his book 'al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh' to researching the determination of the meaning of knowledge and conjecture, and he also devoted the fourth chapter to research on speculative reasoning, proof, and indicator (4). Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) also dealt with specific parts of the theory of knowledge in some parts of his book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql' or 'Muwafaqat Sahih al-Manqul li Sarih al-Ma'qul'. He presented issues of knowledge and speculative reasoning (1), and even defined the paths of knowledge precisely and clearly (2). All this while his book as a whole deals with the core of the theory of knowledge as it discusses the hypothesis of a conflict between reason and revelation, and concludes the unity of knowledge in sources and methods. As for 'Adud al-Din al-Iji (d. 756 AH), he made the first stance of his book 'al-Mawaqif' in 'Ilm al-Kalam' specialized for knowledge and speculative reasoning, and in it he discussed the definition of knowledge and its divisions, speculative reasoning, and whether it yields knowledge (3). He also returned to speak about knowledge and its divisions, its opposites from ignorance, and the verification of whether the perceptions of the senses are knowledge or not? (4). So all this confirms that John Locke – it seems – benefited from those before him a little or a lot, and whether those from whom he benefited were Muslims or non-Muslims, the principle of the unity of knowledge or its accumulation is an epistemic gain for every researcher.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument defending the priority of Muslim scholars in the field of epistemology. The author begins by stating that research into knowledge and its elements is not an invention of John Locke, although Locke's presentation is notable for its systematic organization. Then, the author lists several Muslim scholars and their works to demonstrate their contributions. Al-Baqillani discussed the reality of knowledge, divided sciences into necessary and theoretical, and addressed the sources of knowledge. Al-Baghdadi similarly covered definitions and divisions of sciences, senses, and the concept of mutawatir report. Al-Razi in his Usul al-Fiqh work treated knowledge and conjecture, and also speculative reasoning, proof, and indicators. Ibn Taymiyya's 'Dar' Ta'arud' dealt with issues of knowledge and speculative reasoning, and clearly defined the paths of knowledge, ultimately arguing for the unity of knowledge from reason and revelation. Al-Iji in his 'al-Mawaqif' devoted the first stance to knowledge and speculative reasoning, discussing its definition, divisions, and whether sense perceptions count as knowledge. The author concludes that Locke likely benefited from predecessors, Muslim or otherwise, and emphasizes the cumulative nature of knowledge. The passage uses historical examples to support its claim, but does not present a formal logical argument.

Introduction and Front Matter

Critique of Binary Classification of Knowledge
Author critiques strict binary division between rational and transmitted sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically evaluate binary classifications of knowledge in Islamic thought and identify evidence that challenges such dichotomies.

Core Idea
The division between rational and transmitted sciences is technical, not fundamental; great scholars mastered both.

This lesson examines a critique of the strict binary classification between rational (ʿaqlī) and transmitted (naqlī) sciences, often attributed to Ibn Khaldun. The author presents a researcher's critical questions and then refutes the idea that this division implies a conflict or mutual exclusion. Through examples of al-Ghazali, Ibn Rushd, and Ibn Taymiyya, the lesson demonstrates that the greatest Islamic scholars integrated both domains. You will learn to identify and challenge oversimplified dichotomies in knowledge classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the binary classification being criticized
Read the passage to identify the strict division between 'rational sciences' (ʿulūm al-ʿaql) and 'transmitted sciences' (ʿulūm al-naql) as presented by the researcher. Note how this division is portrayed as mutually exclusive.
The researcher questions why Ibn Khaldun separated the sciences and barred reason from transmitted sciences.
2
Understand the researcher's questions and the author's response
Analyze the series of rhetorical questions posed by the researcher and then track how the author counters them. The author denies that Ibn Khaldun's division was due to perceived contradiction or avoidance of conflict.
The author asserts the division was an 'internal technical classification' (taṣnīf fannī dākhilī).
3
Examine the evidence: the three scholars
Focus on the examples of al-Ghazali, Ibn Rushd, and Ibn Taymiyya. Notice how each scholar is shown to have mastery in both transmitted and rational sciences, despite their famous disagreements.
Al-Ghazali wrote 'Iḥyāʾ ʿUlūm al-Dīn' (transmitted) and engaged with philosophy; Ibn Rushd was a Maliki jurist and philosopher; Ibn Taymiyya criticized logic but not reason per se.
4
Formulate the author's main thesis
Synthesize the author's argument: the division is technical, not ontological; rational and transmitted sciences converge and complement each other. The differences lie in methods, not in subject matter or legitimacy.
The author concludes: 'sciences of revelation and sciences of reason converge and do not diverge.'
5
Apply the critique to avoid oversimplifications
Consider how this critique can be applied to other binary classifications in Islamic thought (e.g., 'Islamic vs. secular', 'traditional vs. modern'). Use the author's method: test the classification against real-world examples of integration.
This step helps you use the lesson's tool in broader contexts.

وفي سياق التقليل من أهمية تصنيف المعرفة باعتماد الوحي قسماً رئيساً لها، يتساءل أحد الباحثين مستنكراً على ابن خلدون تصنيفه آنف الذكر قائلاً: لماذا أوقع ابن خلدون هذا الفصل بين العلوم؟ ولماذا حجب العقل عن التدخل في علوم النقل؟ هل لأنه كان يفترض وجود تناقض بينه وبينها؟ ولماذا حصر سند العلوم النقلية في الرواية فقط؟ وخص العلوم العقلية بالبرهان؟ وهل كانت علوم النقل قد بلغت الغاية التي لا شيء فوقها حقاً؟ أم أنه كان يتفادى الاصطدام. من يقف وراء هذه العلوم ويتكلم باسمها؟ لا أحد يملك جواباً مقنعاً لهذه الأسئلة، غير أن هذا الفصل بين علوم النقل وعلوم العقل كانت له نتيجة حسنة بالنسبة إلى العلوم العقلية، لعلّ الرجل يكون قد عناها، فقد وفر لها - عن طريق الاعتراف بقدسية العلوم الأخرى - فرص الحماية من تدخل أهل النقل فيها. وبهذا ضمن لها - أو هكذا ظن - فرص الحرية للمضي في طريقها إلى اكتشاف الكون الطبيعي، واستقراء قوانينه ونواميسه، وتحليل ظواهره بتشخيص أسبابها وعللها من دون تدخل الغيبيين وتجريحاتهم. والحق أن فصل ابن خلدون بين علوم العقل وعلوم الوحي أو النقل لم يكن لوجود تناقض بينها. تماماً كما ليس لأنه كان يتفادى الاصطدام بمن يقف وراء علوم النقل ... ولكنه كان تصنيفاً فنياً داخلياً دفعه الفصل الموضوعي بين العلوم لا حجب بعضها عن بعض كلية بحيث يمكن أن نطلق مصطلح (أهل النقل) على علماء الشريعة وحدهم، وأهل العقل) على علماء الفلسفة والمنطق فقط، بصورة فصل لا لقاء فيه بين الطرفين كما هو ظاهر المصطلحين في سياق حديث هذا الباحث. لا يصح ذلك؛ لأن جمهرة واسعة من (أهل النقل) كانت على معرفة ممتازة بعلوم الفلسفة والمنطق. ومثلهم (أهل العقل) فإن جمهورهم كان على علم واسع بعلوم الشريعة بشتى فروعها. وما بدا من خلاف علمي بين العلماء وبعضهم فأياً ما بلغت حدته لا يرقى إلى تصنيفه بأنه خلاف تيارات متضادة، وليس أدل على هذا من أن علماء كالغزالي (ت: 505هـ) وابن تيمية (728هـ) وابن رشد الحفيد (ت: 595هـ) - على سبيل المثال - تعجز عن تصنيفهم في جانب فريق دون الآخر، إذ على الرغم من حدة الخلاف وشهرته بين أبي حامد الغزالي صاحب (تهافت الفلاسفة) وأبي الوليد محمد بن أحمد بن رشد صاحب (تهافت التهافت) فإن أحدا لا ينكر أن منهما كان فيلسوفاً في حين فقيهاً، أي عقلياً ونقلياً في آن واحد. كان الغزالي صاحب كتاب (إحياء علوم الدين) الذي جمع فيه علوم الشريعة فأوعى، وكان ابن رشد صاحب رسالة فصل المقال فيما بين الشريعة والحكمة من الاتصال، وكان هذا الثاني فقيهاً لا يشق له غبار، بدليل كتابه الشهير والذي بات عمدة في الفقه المقارن بداية المجتهد ونهاية المقتصد) علاوة على أن ابن رشد فقيه مالكي معدود في أقطاب علماء المالكية. وتستطيع أن تجد الأمر ذاته لدى عالم كابن تيمية، فعلى حين تصدى للفلاسفة والمنطقيين على نحو لربما يشعر لأول وهله أنه كان ضد هذين العلمين جملة وتفصيلاً، ولكنه إنما حارب نصبهما أمام (السمع) أو (النقل) كما لو كانا في طرف مضاد له. وحقيقة الأمر - عنده - أن علوم السمع وعلوم العقل تلتقي ولا تفترق وتتفق ولا تختلف وإنما أساليب وطرائق الفلاسفة والمتكلمين
In the context of downplaying the importance of classifying knowledge by making revelation a primary division thereof, one researcher asks, criticizing Ibn Khaldun's aforementioned classification, saying: Why did Ibn Khaldun make this division between the sciences? Why did he prevent the intellect from interfering in the transmitted sciences? Was it because he assumed a contradiction between them? Why did he restrict the basis of the transmitted sciences to narration alone and specify the rational sciences with demonstration? Had the transmitted sciences truly reached a point beyond which nothing? Or was he avoiding collision with those behind these sciences who speak in their name? No one has a convincing answer to these questions, but this division between transmitted and rational sciences had a favorable result for the rational sciences—perhaps the man intended it—for it provided them, by acknowledging the sanctity of the other sciences, opportunities for protection from interference by the people of transmission. Thus he guaranteed them—or so he thought—opportunities for freedom to proceed on their path to discovering the natural universe, inductively ascertaining its laws and regularities, analyzing its phenomena by diagnosing their causes and reasons without interference from the supernaturalists and their criticisms. The truth is that Ibn Khaldun's division between rational sciences and the sciences of revelation or transmission was not due to a contradiction between them. Nor was it because he was avoiding collision with those behind the transmitted sciences... Rather, it was an internal technical classification driven by the objective distinction between the sciences, not a total separation between them, such that one could apply the term 'people of transmission' solely to scholars of the sacred law and 'people of intellect' solely to philosophers and logicians, in a manner of separation with no meeting between the two sides, as appears from the terms in this researcher's discussion. This is not correct, because a vast multitude of the 'people of transmission' had excellent knowledge of the sciences of philosophy and logic. Similarly, the 'people of intellect,' the majority of them had extensive knowledge of the religious sciences in all their branches. Whatever scientific disagreement appeared between some scholars, no matter how intense, it does not reach the level of being classified as a conflict of opposing currents. Nothing indicates this more than that scholars such as al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH), Ibn Taymiyya (728 AH), and Ibn Rushd the Grandson (d. 595 AH)—for example—cannot be classified on one side without the other. For despite the intensity and fame of the dispute between Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, author of 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers,' and Abu al-Walid Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Rushd, author of 'The Incoherence of the Incoherence,' no one denies that each of them was a philosopher at times and a jurist at others, i.e., rationalist and traditionalist simultaneously. Al-Ghazali was the author of 'The Revival of the Religious Sciences,' in which he collected and comprehensively covered the religious sciences, and Ibn Rushd was the author of the treatise 'Decisive Treatise on the Connection between the Sacred Law and Philosophy,' and this latter was a jurist of unequalled expertise, as evidenced by his famous book which became a fundamental reference in comparative jurisprudence, 'The Beginning of the Mujtahid and the End of the Moderate,' in addition to Ibn Rushd being a Maliki jurist counted among the leading scholars of the Maliki school. And you can find the same with a scholar like Ibn Taymiyya. While he confronted the philosophers and logicians in a way that might first give the impression that he was against these two sciences entirely and in detail, he actually only fought their setting themselves up in opposition to revelation or transmission, as if they were on an opposing side. The truth of the matter—according to him—is that the sciences of revelation and the sciences of reason converge and do not diverge, agree and do not differ, it is only the methods and ways of the philosophers and theologians.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage critiques the simplistic binary classification of knowledge into rational (ʿaqlī) and transmitted (naqlī) sciences, as adopted by some researchers in response to Ibn Khaldun's classification. The author first presents a researcher's critical questions to Ibn Khaldun: why separate the sciences, bar reason from transmitted sciences, restrict transmitted sciences to narration and rational sciences to demonstration? The author then explains that Ibn Khaldun's division was not based on contradiction or avoidance but was an internal technical classification driven by objective distinctions. The author argues that the division should not be taken as absolute, because many scholars of transmission (like jurists and hadith experts) were well-versed in philosophy and logic, and vice versa. The passage uses three key examples: al-Ghazali, Ibn Rushd, and Ibn Taymiyya. Al-Ghazali, despite his famous critique of philosophers in 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers,' was also a master of religious sciences as shown in 'The Revival of the Religious Sciences.' Ibn Rushd, who defended philosophy in 'The Decisive Treatise,' was a leading Maliki jurist and author of 'Bidayat al-Mujtahid.' Ibn Taymiyya, known for his fierce critique of logic and philosophy, only opposed their opposition to revelation (samʿ/naql), not the sciences themselves. The author concludes that the sound understanding is that transmitted and rational sciences converge and agree; the differences are only in methods and approaches of philosophers and theologians. This passage is a critical analysis of epistemological classification, emphasizing integration over division.

علوم العقل
البرهان

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Quranic Command to Read: Foundation of Islamic Epistemology and Education
Author introduces Quranic epistemological framework and argues for the centrality of reading

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the command 'Iqra' is considered the foundational act for knowledge acquisition in Islam, and identify the three epistemological principles for understanding the Quran presented by the author.

Core Idea
Reading is the key to all knowledge; Islam began with 'Iqra' because without reading, neither knowledge nor civilization can exist.

This passage from a contemporary Islamic epistemological work makes a bold claim: the very first word of the final revelation was 'Iqra' (Read), establishing reading as the indispensable foundation for all knowledge, education, and civilization. The author argues that reading is not merely a skill but an epistemological principle rooted in the Quran itself, linking revelation, the universe, and human cognition. In this lesson, you will unpack the author's arguments and see how this Quranic starting point shapes the Islamic worldview on knowledge and education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles for understanding the Quran
The author lists three principles: (1) grasping the Quran's language in a way that extends from the time of revelation to modern understanding through contextual reading; (2) the structural unity of the Quran as one objective whole; (3) combining the reading of revelation and the reading of the universe. Note the fourth point about conceptual reading, which adds that Quranic language has a universal dimension not tied to a single fixed meaning.
These principles guide how to approach the Quran as a source of knowledge.
2
Recognize the organic link between reading and knowledge
The author states that education is built on systematic interconnections, and that reading is organically linked to knowledge as a prerequisite. Without reading, knowledge is deficient to the point of not being true knowledge, and civilization cannot arise. Look for the phrase 'reading is the key to knowledge' and the assertion that this is a civilizational law.
The author uses strong language to emphasize that reading is not optional but essential.
3
Analyze the significance of 'Iqra' as the first revelation
The author highlights that Islam began with the command 'Read' before any acts of worship. This is because reading, writing, and learning are the 'sum of all matters' and the 'key to understanding this religion'. The author cites Quranic verses (al-Alaq 96:1-4, al-Qalam 68:1, al-Tur 52:3) to show the link between knowledge, the pen, and writing.
This point underscores the priority of knowledge in Islam.
4
Understand the role of human distinctiveness (bayan) in education
The author notes that God distinguished humans with eloquence (bayan) and intelligence, making them capable of reflection, perception, memory, and imagination. These cognitive abilities produce knowledge. Therefore, one of education's main goals is acquiring knowledge and reaching truth. The passage also discusses the function of knowledge in education from cognitive psychology and philosophy of education.
This connects the epistemological framework to practical educational aims.

تقوم عملية التربية على جملة من الأبنية النظامية المتسمة بالترابط والتسلسل والمنطقية. فثمة علاقة عضوية - على سبيل المثال - تربط المعرفة بالقراءة كخطوة أولى على طريق المسار التربوي، بحيث يصبح من نافلة القول التأكيد على أن القراءة مفتاح المعرفة، وأن المعرفة من غير القراءة ناقصة إلى حد يكاد يسقطها من وصف المعرفة - فضلاً عن المعرفة التربوية -. ومن غير المتصوّر قيام حضارة من غير امتلاك لمهارة القراءة، ومن ثم امتلاك لناصية المعرفة، إذ إن ذلك يخالف سنة حضارية لا فكاك منها. ومنذ بدأ الإنسان يقرأ ويكتب فقد بدأ يعرف ويعلم، وأضحى كل يوم في نماء معرفي مطرد باطراد قراءته (۲). ونظراً لما للقراءة من أهمية معرفية فقد بدأت رسالة الإسلام الخاتمة بكلمة (اقرأ)، ولم تبدأ بالأمر بالصوم، أو الصلاة، أو الزكاة، أو الجهاد، برغم أهمية ذلك كله في بناء الإسلام. وما ذلك إلا لأن القراءة والكتابة والتعلم جماع الأمر كله، ومفتاح فهم هذا الدين (۱) . إن هذا الأمر بمثابة إلغاء لعهد الأمية، وفتح لعهد جديد، عهد واقرأ (العلق: ١/٩٦]، وعَلَّمَ بِالْقَلَمِ [العلق: ٤/٩٦]، ﴾ وون وَالْقَلَمِ وَمَا يَسْطُرُونَ ﴾ [القلم: ١/٦٨)، وعهد فِي رَقٌ مَنشور الطور ٣/٥٢] هذه الكلمات التي ربطت المعرفة بالقراءة والكتابة، والقلم وما يسطرون(٢).

Chapter One, Section One - Development of Epistemology
1- Grasping the nature of the Quran's language in a way that extends from the horizon of the Bedouin understanding with which the Arab dealt at the time of the Quran's revelation to the horizon of knowledge in our time, according to a contextual reading of the verses that makes the Quran itself the most important source for understanding it.
2- The structural unity of the Quran, meaning that it is one body, read in its own context, as an objective unit read comprehensively.
3- Combining the reading of revelation and the reading of the universe.
- Conceptual reading: meaning that the language of the Quran has a conceptual dimension suitable for every time and place, and its meaning is not required to remain fixed across ages and in various regions. (1)
The Relationship of Knowledge to Education
Education is based on a set of systematic structures characterized by interconnection, sequence, and logic. There is, for example, an organic relationship linking knowledge to reading as a first step on the educational path, such that it becomes superfluous to emphasize that reading is the key to knowledge, and that knowledge without reading is deficient to the point that it almost ceases to be described as knowledge—let alone educational knowledge. It is unimaginable to build a civilization without possessing the skill of reading, and then possessing the essence of knowledge, for that violates an inescapable civilizational law. Since man began to read and write, he began to know and learn, and each day he grew in knowledge commensurate with his reading (2). Due to the cognitive importance of reading, the final message of Islam began with the word 'Read' (Iqra), and did not begin with the command to fast, or pray, or give charity, or strive, despite the importance of all that in building Islam. That is only because reading, writing, and learning are the sum of all matters and the key to understanding this religion (1). This matter is like an abolition of the era of illiteracy and the opening of a new era, the era of 'Read' (al-Alaq 96:1), 'He taught by the pen' (al-Alaq 96:4), 'Nun, by the pen and what they inscribe' (al-Qalam 68:1), and the era of 'in a parchment unfolded' (al-Tur 52:3)—these words that linked knowledge to reading, writing, the pen, and what they inscribe (2).
And if God Almighty created man and distinguished him with eloquence: 'The Most Merciful, taught the Quran, created man, taught him eloquence' (al-Rahman 55:1-4), and also distinguished him with intelligence, as he reflects, perceives, remembers, and imagines, so that his mental activities become a distinctive cognitive product for him among all other creatures, then one of the main goals of education becomes his acquisition of knowledge (3) and reaching the truth, because the role of science depends on the type of view regarding the place of science in the field of knowledge.
The function of knowledge for education is determined from the perspective of cognitive psychology as overcoming perceptual or sensory defects by providing internal motivation, which begins outside things, so that perception is bound by knowledge in order not to be exposed to various types of deception. From the perspective of philosophy of education, the function of knowledge is determined educationally by education's use of philosophical investigations in epistemology in order to achieve the basic task of education (teaching) in determining the stance on knowledge as source, means, nature, and possibility (3). Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition from a contemporary Islamic work, establishing the Quranic foundations of knowledge and its relationship to education. The author begins by outlining three principles for understanding the Quran: first, that the language of the Quran has a dynamic meaning that extends from the time of revelation to modern understanding through contextual reading; second, that the Quran has a structural unity as an objective whole; third, that it combines the reading of revelation and the reading of the universe. A fourth point introduces 'conceptual reading', emphasizing that Quranic language has a universal conceptual dimension not tied to a single fixed meaning across time. The author then transitions to the relationship between knowledge and education, arguing that reading is foundational. He states that education is built on systematic interconnections, and that reading is organically linked to knowledge as a prerequisite. Without reading, knowledge is deficient, and civilization cannot arise. The author emphasizes that the Islamic revelation began with the command 'Iqra' (Read) before any acts of worship, because reading, writing, and learning are the key to understanding the entire religion. He cites Quranic verses to support the link between knowledge, the pen, and writing. The passage then discusses human distinctiveness: God created man with eloquence and intelligence, enabling cognitive activities. Hence, education should aim for knowledge and truth. Finally, the author discusses the function of knowledge in education from two perspectives: cognitive psychology (overcoming perceptual errors through internal motivation) and philosophy of education (using epistemology to determine the stance on knowledge's source, means, nature, and possibility).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason Islam began with the command 'Iqra' (Read) rather than with commands for prayer, fasting, or charity?
A) Because reading was an easier practice for the early Muslims to adopt.
B) Because reading, writing, and learning are the foundation and key to understanding the entire religion.
C) Because the Prophet Muhammad was illiterate and needed to learn first.
D) Because other acts of worship were not revealed until later in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's first command 'Iqra' establishes reading as the foundational act for all knowledge, education, and civilization, reflecting an epistemological framework where literacy and learning are prerequisites for understanding revelation and the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short explanation of how the three principles (contextual understanding, structural unity, combining revelation and universe) can be applied in designing a modern Islamic studies curriculum.
?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Quranic Foundation of Knowledge
Quranic basis for knowledge in Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic verses cited as the basis for knowledge and explain their significance for Islamic education.

Core Idea
Reading, writing, and learning are the keys to understanding Islam.

This passage opens by asserting that reading, writing, and learning are central to Islam. The author cites several Quranic verses that link knowledge to the act of reading and the pen, establishing a new era of literacy. Understanding these verses helps us see how Islamic education is rooted in divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic references
Find the verses mentioned: 'Read' (Al-Alaq 96:1), 'He taught by the pen' (Al-Alaq 96:4), 'Nun. By the pen' (Al-Qalam 68:1), and 'in a parchment unrolled' (At-Tur 52:3). Notice how these all emphasize the written word.
2
Identify the core message
The author argues that these verses abolish the era of illiteracy and open a new era. What does this imply about the role of knowledge in Islam?
3
Connect knowledge to human distinction
The passage notes that Allah distinguished humans with expression (bayan) and intelligence. How does this relate to the purpose of education?
4
Summarize the argument
In your own words, state why the author believes reading and writing are fundamental to understanding Islam.

النظريات التربوية المختلفة في كيفية الوصول إلى معرفة الحقيقة بين مغال في الاعتماد على الحس، أفضى به إلى الاعتقاد بعدم الحاجة إلى صوغ نظرية تربوية في المعرفة، وبين معتدل يركز اهتمامه على المشاركة Chapter One, Section One: The Development of Epistemology in the Construction of Islam. And that is only because reading, writing, and learning are the sum total of the matter, and the key to understanding this religion (1). Indeed, this matter is tantamount to abolishing the era of illiteracy, and opening a new era, the era of 'Read' (Al-Alaq 96:1), and 'He taught by the pen' (Al-Alaq 96:4), and 'Nun. By the pen and what they inscribe' (Al-Qalam 68:1), and the era of 'in a parchment unrolled' (At-Tur 52:3). These words that linked knowledge to reading and writing, and the pen and what they inscribe (2). And since God Almighty created man and distinguished him with expression: 'The Most Merciful, taught the Quran, created man, taught him expression' (Ar-Rahman 55:1-4), and also distinguished him with intelligence as he reflects, perceives, remembers, and imagines, so his mental activities become a distinctive cognitive product for him among all creatures, then one of the main goals of education becomes his acquisition of knowledge (3), and attaining truth, because the role of knowledge depends on the type of view of the place of knowledge in the field of epistemology. And the function of knowledge with respect to education from the perspective of cognitive psychology is determined in overcoming perceptual or sensory deficiencies by providing internal motivation, which begins outside things, so perception is bound by knowledge so that it is not exposed to various types of deception (). As for from the perspective of philosophy of education, the function of knowledge educationally is determined by education's use of philosophical investigations in epistemology so that the basic task of education (teaching) is achieved in determining the stance towards knowledge as source, means, nature, and possibility (3). And although there are different ways to attain truth, the stance towards knowing it remains linked to education's stance towards knowledge regardless of the stance of different educational theories on how to reach knowledge of truth, between one excessive in reliance on the senses, which led him to believe in no need to formulate an educational theory of knowledge, and one moderate who focuses his attention on the active participation of the learner in the nature of the studied thing (1). And because of these differences, there are two main trends among educators in their view of the relationship between knowledge and education. These two trends are: The first trend considers knowledge as power, given that the main goal of education is the acquisition of knowledge, and the subject matter possesses intrinsic value because of the knowledge it contains that helps the student discover truth, and this is the view of essentialist and perennialist philosophy. The second trend: It considers knowledge as a result of thinking, on the basis that the sole goal of education is to help the student think, and that is the doctrine of instrumental pragmatism and progressivism which states that knowledge is nothing but a result of thinking (3). Perhaps the reason for that disagreement is due to three main causes: The first cause: That epistemology represents the basic key to understanding education in any philosophical trend, so the difference of multiple trends is but an emanation from differences in epistemologies, because they affect the intellectual structure of each trend, so issues of the universe, man's relationship to it, human nature, goals, values, and others come as extensions of its epistemology (3). Nadia Jamal al-Din, Knowledge in the Four Trends, p. 133.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an introductory front matter from a modern work on Islamic education and epistemology. It opens by asserting that reading, writing, and learning are central to Islam, referencing key Quranic verses that command reading and mention the pen. The author then discusses that God distinguished humans with expression (bayan) and intelligence, making cognitive activity unique to humans, so acquiring knowledge is a primary educational goal. Next, the function of knowledge is examined from two perspectives: cognitive psychology (overcoming perceptual flaws) and philosophy of education (determining stance on knowledge). The passage then notes that there are different ways to truth, but the educational stance on knowledge remains crucial, leading to two main trends among educators: the first considers knowledge as power (essentialist/perennialist), the second considers knowledge as a result of thinking (pragmatist/progressivist). Finally, the author identifies three causes for this disagreement, the first being that epistemology is key to understanding any educational philosophy, and differences in epistemology lead to differences in all other philosophical issues. The passage serves to contextualize the study of epistemology in Islamic education.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic emphasis on reading and writing establishes knowledge acquisition as a foundational goal of Islamic education.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Two Trends in Educational Epistemology
Two educational trends on knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two main trends regarding the nature of knowledge in education and identify the root cause of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Epistemology shapes education: knowledge is either a power to be acquired or the result of thinking.

The passage introduces two competing educational philosophies: one sees knowledge as a valuable asset to be transmitted (essentialism/perennialism), the other sees knowledge as the outcome of active thinking (pragmatism/progressivism). Understanding these helps clarify debates in Islamic educational thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two trends
The first trend views knowledge as power, with education focused on acquiring subject matter. The second views knowledge as a result of thinking, with education focused on helping the student think.
2
Understand the philosophical labels
The first trend is associated with essentialism and perennialism; the second with instrumental pragmatism and progressivism. What values does each philosophy prioritize?
3
Analyze the root cause of disagreement
The author lists three causes, but focuses on the first: epistemology is the key to understanding any philosophy, and differences in epistemology lead to differences in all other issues.
4
Apply to Islamic context
Consider how these trends might manifest in Islamic education: for example, traditional madrasa education emphasizing memorization (knowledge as power) vs. modern approaches emphasizing ijtihad (knowledge as thinking).

الباب الأول: الفصل الأول - تطوّر نظرية المعرفة في بناء الإسلام. وما ذلك إلا لأن القراءة والكتابة والتعلم جماع الأمر كله، ومفتاح فهم هذا الدين (۱) . إن هذا الأمر بمثابة إلغاء لعهد الأمية، وفتح لعهد جديد، عهد واقرأ (العلق: ١/٩٦]، وعَلَّمَ بِالْقَلَمِ [العلق: ٤/٩٦]، ﴾ ن وَالْقَلَمِ وَمَا يَسْطُرُونَ ﴾ [القلم: ١/٦٨)، وعهد فِي رَقٌ مَنشور الطور ٣/٥٢] هذه الكلمات التي ربطت المعرفة بالقراءة والكتابة، والقلم وما يسطرون(٢). وإذا كان الله تعالى قد خلق الإنسان وميّزه بالبيان الرَّحْمَنُ، عَلْمَ الْقُرْآنَ، خَلَقَ الإِنْسانَ عَلَّمَهُ الْبَيان ﴾ [الرحمن: ١/٥٥-٤)، كما ميزه بالذكاء أيضاً حيث يتأمل ويدرك ويتذكر ويتخيل، فتصبح أنشطته العقلية نتاجاً معرفياً مميزاً لـه مـن بين سائر الكائنات، فإن أحد الأهداف الرئيسة للتربية يغدو اكتسابه للمعرفة(۳)، وبلوغ الحقيقة)، لأن دور العلم يتوقف على نوع النظرة إلى مكان العلم في ميدان المعرفة). وتتحدد وظيفة المعرفة بالنسبة للتربية من وجهة علم النفس المعرفي في التغلب على العيوب الإداركية أو الحسية عن طريق توفير الباعث الداخلي، الذي يبدأ خارج الأشياء، فيتقيد الإدراك بالمعرفة كي لا يتعرض لمختلف أنواع الخداع () . أما من وجهة فلسفة التربية فتتحدد وظيفة المعرفة تربوياً باستعانة التربية بالمباحث الفلسفية في نظرية المعرفة كي تتحقق المهمة الأساسية للتربية (التعليم) في تحديد الموقف من المعرفة مصدراً ووسيلة وطبيعة وإمكاناً (۳). ومع أن هناك طرقاً مختلفة لبلوغ الحقيقة، إلا أن الموقف من معرفتها يظل مرتبطاً بموقف التربية من المعرفة بصرف النظر عن موقف النظريات التربوية المختلفة في كيفية الوصول إلى معرفة الحقيقة بين مغال في الاعتماد على الحس، أفضى به إلى الاعتقاد بعدم الحاجة إلى صوغ نظرية تربوية في المعرفة، وبين معتدل يركز اهتمامه على المشاركة الفاعلة للمتعلم في طبيعة الشيء المدروس (1) وبسبب من هذه الاختلافات فئمة اتجاهان رئيسان عند المربين في نظرتهم إلى علاقة المعرفة بالتربية وهذان الاتجاهان هما: الاتجاه الأول يعدّ المعرفة قوة، وذلك بالنظر إلى أن الهدف الرئيس من التربية هو تحصيل المعرفة والمادة الدراسية تمتلك قيمة ذاتية بما تتضمنه من معرفة تساعد التلميذ على اكتشاف الحقيقة وهذه هي نظرة الفلسفة الجوهرية والتواترية. الاتجاه الثاني: ويعد المعرفة محصلة للتفكير، وذلك من منطلق أن هدف التربية الوحيد هو مساعدة التلميذ على التفكير، وذلك مذهب البراجماتية الذرائعية والتقدمية القائلة بأن المعرفة ليست إلا محصلة للتفكير (۳). ولعل مردّ ذلك الخلاف Chapter One, Section One: The Development of Epistemology in the Construction of Islam. And that is only because reading, writing, and learning are the sum total of the matter, and the key to understanding this religion (1). Indeed, this matter is tantamount to abolishing the era of illiteracy, and opening a new era, the era of 'Read' (Al-Alaq 96:1), and 'He taught by the pen' (Al-Alaq 96:4), and 'Nun. By the pen and what they inscribe' (Al-Qalam 68:1), and the era of 'in a parchment unrolled' (At-Tur 52:3). These words that linked knowledge to reading and writing, and the pen and what they inscribe (2). And since God Almighty created man and distinguished him with expression: 'The Most Merciful, taught the Quran, created man, taught him expression' (Ar-Rahman 55:1-4), and also distinguished him with intelligence as he reflects, perceives, remembers, and imagines, so his mental activities become a distinctive cognitive product for him among all creatures, then one of the main goals of education becomes his acquisition of knowledge (3), and attaining truth, because the role of knowledge depends on the type of view of the place of knowledge in the field of epistemology. And the function of knowledge with respect to education from the perspective of cognitive psychology is determined in overcoming perceptual or sensory deficiencies by providing internal motivation, which begins outside things, so perception is bound by knowledge so that it is not exposed to various types of deception (). As for from the perspective of philosophy of education, the function of knowledge educationally is determined by education's use of philosophical investigations in epistemology so that the basic task of education (teaching) is achieved in determining the stance towards knowledge as source, means, nature, and possibility (3). And although there are different ways to attain truth, the stance towards knowing it remains linked to education's stance towards knowledge regardless of the stance of different educational theories on how to reach knowledge of truth, between one excessive in reliance on the senses, which led him to believe in no need to formulate an educational theory of knowledge, and one moderate who focuses his attention on the active participation of the learner in the nature of the studied thing (1). And because of these differences, there are two main trends among educators in their view of the relationship between knowledge and education. These two trends are: The first trend considers knowledge as power, given that the main goal of education is the acquisition of knowledge, and the subject matter possesses intrinsic value because of the knowledge it contains that helps the student discover truth, and this is the view of essentialist and perennialist philosophy. The second trend: It considers knowledge as a result of thinking, on the basis that the sole goal of education is to help the student think, and that is the doctrine of instrumental pragmatism and progressivism which states that knowledge is nothing but a result of thinking (3). Perhaps the reason for that disagreement is due to three main causes: The first cause: That epistemology represents the basic key to understanding education in any philosophical trend, so the difference of multiple trends is but an emanation from differences in epistemologies, because they affect the intellectual structure of each trend, so issues of the universe, man's relationship to it, human nature, goals, values, and others come as extensions of its epistemology (3). Nadia Jamal al-Din, Knowledge in the Four Trends, p. 133.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an introductory front matter from a modern work on Islamic education and epistemology. It opens by asserting that reading, writing, and learning are central to Islam, referencing key Quranic verses that command reading and mention the pen. The author then discusses that God distinguished humans with expression (bayan) and intelligence, making cognitive activity unique to humans, so acquiring knowledge is a primary educational goal. Next, the function of knowledge is examined from two perspectives: cognitive psychology (overcoming perceptual flaws) and philosophy of education (determining stance on knowledge). The passage then notes that there are different ways to truth, but the educational stance on knowledge remains crucial, leading to two main trends among educators: the first considers knowledge as power (essentialist/perennialist), the second considers knowledge as a result of thinking (pragmatist/progressivist). Finally, the author identifies three causes for this disagreement, the first being that epistemology is key to understanding any educational philosophy, and differences in epistemology lead to differences in all other philosophical issues. The passage serves to contextualize the study of epistemology in Islamic education.

Key Takeaway
The two main trends in educational epistemology — knowledge as power vs. knowledge as thinking — stem from deeper philosophical commitments about the nature of knowledge itself.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Distinguishing Epistemology from Theory of Knowledge in the French Tradition
Author distinguishes epistemology from theory of knowledge in French tradition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the French concept of epistemology (as philosophy of science) from the broader theory of knowledge, using Lalande's definition.

Core Idea
In French, epistemology is not theory of knowledge; it is the critical philosophy of science.

When reading works on Islamic epistemology, you often encounter terms like 'epistemology' and 'theory of knowledge' used interchangeably. However, this passage reveals a crucial French distinction that influences how Muslim scholars engage with Western thought. By understanding this distinction, you gain a sharper tool for analyzing how modern Islamic thinkers define knowledge and science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Locate the Arabic terms الإبستمولوجيا and نظرية المعرفة in the passage. Notice how the author immediately flags a difference between English and French usage.
The passage explicitly says that in English they are synonymous, but in French they are distinct.
2
Extract Lalande's definition of epistemology
Find where the author quotes Lalande. It says epistemology is the critical study of the principles, hypotheses, and results of sciences, aiming to determine their logical origin, value, and objective scope. It is not methodology, nor a speculative synthesis.
This is a descriptive definition (rasm), not a strict logical hadd.
3
Contrast epistemology with theory of knowledge
The author states that theory of knowledge is more general, investigating nature, limits, and possibility of knowledge on the level of unity of thought, whereas epistemology studies knowledge in detail and posteriorly across various sciences.
Think of theory of knowledge as a broad framework, epistemology as a specialized analysis of scientific knowledge.
4
Note the shift from English usage
The author warns that those who see them as synonymous are following English terminology. In French, they are distinct. This shift affects how you read translated works.
Many Islamic texts in Arabic translate English 'epistemology' as نظرية المعرفة, which can cause confusion.

الباب الأول: الفصل الثاني- التعريفات. التطابق بينهما حيث تصدير الإبستمولوجيا مرادفة لمصطلح نظرية المعرفة تماماً. ويبدو أن جميع الذين رأوا التطابق قائماً بين المعنيين، إنما تابعوا المصطلح الإنجليزي وحده، في حين تختلف الإبستمولوجيا عن نظرية المعرفة في الفرنسية. فالإبستمولوجيا في الفرنسية لا تجعل من المعنيين معنى واحداً، بل تميز بينهما، وتجعل من الإبستمولوجيا مرادفاً لفلسفة العلوم دون أن تكون طريقته، كما يقول (لالاند): إنها تدل على فلسفة العلوم، لكن بمعنى أدق فهي ليست حقاً دراسة المناهج العلمية والتي هي الطرائقية، وتنتمي إلى المنطق. كما أنها ليست توليفاً أو إرهاصاً ظنياً بالقوانين العلمية على منوال المذهب الوضعي والنشوئي). جوهرياً المعلومية هي الدرس النقدي لمبادئ مختلف العلوم وفرضيتها ونتائجها، الرامي إلى تحديد أصلها المنطقي قيمتها ومداها الموضوعي علينا إذن التفريق بين المعلومية ونظرية المعرفة، على الرغم من كون المعلومية مدخلاً لها، ومساعدها اللازب فهي تمتاز عن نظرية المعرفة بأنها تدرس المعرفة بالتفصيل، وبشكل بعدي، في مختلف العلوم والأغراض، أكثر منها تدرسها على صعيد وحدة الفكر. وعلى هذا فإذا شايع بعض الباحثين التفريق بين الإبستمولوجيا وبين نظرية المعرفة، فهم إنما تابعوا المعنى الفرنسي، و(لالاند) على وجه الخصوص. إذن فإنها تبدو وفق هذا المعنى متعارضة مع نظرية المعرفة السائدة اليوم نسبياً، حيث تمثل الإبستمولوجيا مدخلاً لنظرية المعرفة فقط دون أن تعنى شمول جميع عناصرها. وعلى الرغم من أن تمييز الإبستمولوجيا عن نظرية المعرفة بكون الأول - في الفرنسية - يعني (فلسفة العلوم)، إلا أنه حتى هذا المعنى أيضاً يتسم بغموض وعمومية، إذ كل تفكير في أي علم، وفي أي جانب من جوانبه سواء في فروضه أم مبادئه أم قوانينه أم نتائجه، إنما هو بشكل أو بآخر فلسفة لهذا العلم. ويجدر قبل اختتام الحديث عن العلاقة بين نظرية المعرفة و الإبستمولوجيا أن أشير إلى رأيين - في هذا الصدد - ظاهرهما التعارض في إطار المعنى الفرنسي ذاته : الرأي الأول: إنه مهما قيل عن وجود خلاف بين نظرية المعرفة والإبستمولوجيا فإن اتصالاً وثيقاً يربط بينهما من جانب معنى نظرية المعرفة (العام) الذي يبحث في طبيعتها وحدودها وإمكان الحصول عليها، ومن جانب معنى الإبستمولوجيا الدقيق الخاص كمظهر بارز على صعيد التحليل الفلسفي المجرّد أي إن هناك اتصالاً وانفصالاً بينهما، كما أن هناك عمومية أكثر لنظرية المعرفة، وخصوصية أدق للإبستمولوجيا. الرأي الثاني: ويبني رأيه على أساس من تميز نظرية المعرفة في جميع المذاهب الفلسفية عن الإبستمولوجيا، ثم يخلص إلى القول: إن الإبستمولوجيا ليست نظرية في المعرفة؛ لأنها تحاول ألا تربط - مثل نظرية المعرفة - بالأهداف العامة لنسق فلسفي ما. ومن جهة ثانية فإن الإبستمولوجيا ليست جزءاً من نظرية المعرفة خاصاً بالبحث في المعرفة العملية، ذلك لأن الاختلاف الأساسي بين نظرية المعرفة والإبستمولوجيا هو أن الإبستمولوجيا لا هدف لها في بناء نظرية خاصة في المعرفة وأنها تتناول التفكير العلمي في مرحلة تاريخية من مراحل.
First chapter, second section: Definitions. The correspondence between them where epistemology is made synonymous with the term theory of knowledge completely. And it appears that all who saw the correspondence existing between the two meanings only followed the English term alone, whereas epistemology differs from theory of knowledge in French. For epistemology in French does not make the two meanings one, but distinguishes between them, and makes epistemology synonymous with philosophy of science without being its methodology, as Lalande says: It indicates philosophy of science, but in a more precise sense it is not truly the study of scientific methods, which is methodology, and belongs to logic. Nor is it a synthesis or hypothetical anticipation of scientific laws on the model of positivism and evolutionism. Essentially, epistemology is the critical study of the principles of various sciences, their hypotheses and results, aiming to determine their logical origin, value, and objective scope. So we must distinguish between epistemology and theory of knowledge, despite epistemology being an entry point to it and its necessary aid; it is distinguished from theory of knowledge by studying knowledge in detail and posteriorly in various sciences and disciplines, more than studying it on the level of the unity of thought. And thus, if some researchers followed the distinction between epistemology and theory of knowledge, they only followed the French meaning, and Lalande in particular. So according to this meaning, it appears opposed to the theory of knowledge prevailing today relatively, where epistemology represents only an entry point to theory of knowledge without encompassing all its elements. And despite the distinction of epistemology from theory of knowledge by the former—in French—meaning philosophy of science, even this meaning is characterized by ambiguity and generality, for every reflection on any science and any aspect of it, whether in its hypotheses, principles, laws, or results, is in one way or another a philosophy of that science. And before concluding the discussion on the relationship between theory of knowledge and epistemology, I should point out two opinions—in this regard—apparently contradictory within the same French meaning: The first opinion: Whatever is said about the existence of disagreement between theory of knowledge and epistemology, a close connection links them on the side of the general meaning of theory of knowledge that investigates its nature, limits, and the possibility of obtaining it, and on the side of the precise special meaning of epistemology as a prominent manifestation on the level of abstract philosophical analysis; that is, there is connection and separation between them, and there is greater generality for theory of knowledge and more precise specificity for epistemology. The second opinion: It builds its opinion on the basis of the distinction of theory of knowledge in all philosophical schools from epistemology, then concludes by saying: Epistemology is not a theory of knowledge; because it tries not to connect—like theory of knowledge—with the general goals of any philosophical system. And on the other hand, epistemology is not a part of theory of knowledge specific to research in practical knowledge, because the fundamental difference between theory of knowledge and epistemology is that epistemology has no goal of constructing a special theory of knowledge and that it deals with scientific thinking in a historical stage of stages.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion that distinguishes between two related fields: epistemology (الإبستمولوجيا) and theory of knowledge (نظرية المعرفة). The author begins by noting that in English, these terms are often used synonymously, but in French they are distinct. Following the French tradition, particularly philosopher Lalande, epistemology is defined as the philosophy of science—a critical study of the principles, hypotheses, and results of various sciences, aiming to determine their logical origin, value, and objective scope. It is not methodology, nor a speculative synthesis of scientific laws. Epistemology is an entry point and necessary aid to theory of knowledge, but differs by studying knowledge in detail and retrospectively across different sciences, rather than at the level of the unity of thought. The author then presents two contrasting opinions within the French framework. The first opinion holds that despite apparent disagreement, there is a close connection between theory of knowledge and epistemology: theory of knowledge has a general meaning (nature, limits, possibility of knowledge) while epistemology has a precise special meaning (abstract philosophical analysis of scientific knowledge). There is both connection and separation, with theory of knowledge being more general and epistemology more specific. The second opinion argues that epistemology is not a theory of knowledge at all, because it does not aim to build a special theory of knowledge nor tie itself to the general goals of any philosophical system. Its focus is on scientific thinking within a historical stage. The passage thus clarifies the French usage of these terms and highlights the nuances in their relationship.

Introduction and Front Matter

Two Views on the Relationship Between Epistemology and Theory of Knowledge
Author presents two contrasting opinions on the relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the two opinions presented on the relationship between epistemology and theory of knowledge within the French framework.

Core Idea
There is both connection and separation: some see a close link, others see epistemology as independent from theory of knowledge.

After establishing the basic distinction, the author presents two conflicting opinions that emerge from the French tradition itself. These opinions reveal deeper tensions about how to define the scope and aim of epistemology relative to the general theory of knowledge. By analyzing these views, you will appreciate the complexity of defining fields of knowledge and how similar debates occur in Islamic classification of sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two opinions in the passage
Find the section beginning with 'الرأي الأول' and 'الرأي الثاني'. The author explicitly labels them.
They are presented as apparently contradictory within the same French meaning.
2
Summarize the first opinion
The first opinion holds that despite apparent disagreement, there is a close connection: theory of knowledge is general (nature, limits, possibility), epistemology is specific (abstract philosophical analysis of scientific knowledge). There is both connection and separation.
It emphasizes complementarity: epistemology as a special part of the broader theory.
3
Summarize the second opinion
The second opinion argues epistemology is not a theory of knowledge at all, because it avoids linking to general goals of any philosophical system. It is not even a part of theory of knowledge; it deals with scientific thinking historically.
It stresses independence and even opposition to theory of knowledge.
4
Compare the two opinions
Both agree that there is a distinction, but they differ on whether epistemology is a subset of theory of knowledge or entirely separate. The first sees a hierarchical relation; the second sees two different enterprises.
Consider how such debates exist in Islamic sciences, e.g., whether Usul al-Fiqh is a part of Kalam or an independent discipline.

الباب الأول: الفصل الثاني- التعريفات. التطابق بينهما حيث تصدير الإبستمولوجيا مرادفة لمصطلح نظرية المعرفة تماماً. ويبدو أن جميع الذين رأوا التطابق قائماً بين المعنيين، إنما تابعوا المصطلح الإنجليزي وحده، في حين تختلف الإبستمولوجيا عن نظرية المعرفة في الفرنسية. فالإبستمولوجيا في الفرنسية لا تجعل من المعنيين معنى واحداً، بل تميز بينهما، وتجعل من الإبستمولوجيا مرادفاً لفلسفة العلوم دون أن تكون طريقته، كما يقول (لالاند): إنها تدل على فلسفة العلوم، لكن بمعنى أدق فهي ليست حقاً دراسة المناهج العلمية والتي هي الطرائقية، وتنتمي إلى المنطق. كما أنها ليست توليفاً أو إرهاصاً ظنياً بالقوانين العلمية على منوال المذهب الوضعي والنشوئي). جوهرياً المعلومية هي الدرس النقدي لمبادئ مختلف العلوم وفرضيتها ونتائجها، الرامي إلى تحديد أصلها المنطقي قيمتها ومداها الموضوعي علينا إذن التفريق بين المعلومية ونظرية المعرفة، على الرغم من كون المعلومية مدخلاً لها، ومساعدها اللازب فهي تمتاز عن نظرية المعرفة بأنها تدرس المعرفة بالتفصيل، وبشكل بعدي، في مختلف العلوم والأغراض، أكثر منها تدرسها على صعيد وحدة الفكر. وعلى هذا فإذا شايع بعض الباحثين التفريق بين الإبستمولوجيا وبين نظرية المعرفة، فهم إنما تابعوا المعنى الفرنسي، و(لالاند) على وجه الخصوص. إذن فإنها تبدو وفق هذا المعنى متعارضة مع نظرية المعرفة السائدة اليوم نسبياً، حيث تمثل الإبستمولوجيا مدخلاً لنظرية المعرفة فقط دون أن تعنى شمول جميع عناصرها. وعلى الرغم من أن تمييز الإبستمولوجيا عن نظرية المعرفة بكون الأول - في الفرنسية - يعني (فلسفة العلوم)، إلا أنه حتى هذا المعنى أيضاً يتسم بغموض وعمومية، إذ كل تفكير في أي علم، وفي أي جانب من جوانبه سواء في فروضه أم مبادئه أم قوانينه أم نتائجه، إنما هو بشكل أو بآخر فلسفة لهذا العلم. ويجدر قبل اختتام الحديث عن العلاقة بين نظرية المعرفة و الإبستمولوجيا أن أشير إلى رأيين - في هذا الصدد - ظاهرهما التعارض في إطار المعنى الفرنسي ذاته : الرأي الأول: إنه مهما قيل عن وجود خلاف بين نظرية المعرفة والإبستمولوجيا فإن اتصالاً وثيقاً يربط بينهما من جانب معنى نظرية المعرفة (العام) الذي يبحث في طبيعتها وحدودها وإمكان الحصول عليها، ومن جانب معنى الإبستمولوجيا الدقيق الخاص كمظهر بارز على صعيد التحليل الفلسفي المجرّد أي إن هناك اتصالاً وانفصالاً بينهما، كما أن هناك عمومية أكثر لنظرية المعرفة، وخصوصية أدق للإبستمولوجيا. الرأي الثاني: ويبني رأيه على أساس من تميز نظرية المعرفة في جميع المذاهب الفلسفية عن الإبستمولوجيا، ثم يخلص إلى القول: إن الإبستمولوجيا ليست نظرية في المعرفة؛ لأنها تحاول ألا تربط - مثل نظرية المعرفة - بالأهداف العامة لنسق فلسفي ما. ومن جهة ثانية فإن الإبستمولوجيا ليست جزءاً من نظرية المعرفة خاصاً بالبحث في المعرفة العملية، ذلك لأن الاختلاف الأساسي بين نظرية المعرفة والإبستمولوجيا هو أن الإبستمولوجيا لا هدف لها في بناء نظرية خاصة في المعرفة وأنها تتناول التفكير العلمي في مرحلة تاريخية من مراحل.
First chapter, second section: Definitions. The correspondence between them where epistemology is made synonymous with the term theory of knowledge completely. And it appears that all who saw the correspondence existing between the two meanings only followed the English term alone, whereas epistemology differs from theory of knowledge in French. For epistemology in French does not make the two meanings one, but distinguishes between them, and makes epistemology synonymous with philosophy of science without being its methodology, as Lalande says: It indicates philosophy of science, but in a more precise sense it is not truly the study of scientific methods, which is methodology, and belongs to logic. Nor is it a synthesis or hypothetical anticipation of scientific laws on the model of positivism and evolutionism. Essentially, epistemology is the critical study of the principles of various sciences, their hypotheses and results, aiming to determine their logical origin, value, and objective scope. So we must distinguish between epistemology and theory of knowledge, despite epistemology being an entry point to it and its necessary aid; it is distinguished from theory of knowledge by studying knowledge in detail and posteriorly in various sciences and disciplines, more than studying it on the level of the unity of thought. And thus, if some researchers followed the distinction between epistemology and theory of knowledge, they only followed the French meaning, and Lalande in particular. So according to this meaning, it appears opposed to the theory of knowledge prevailing today relatively, where epistemology represents only an entry point to theory of knowledge without encompassing all its elements. And despite the distinction of epistemology from theory of knowledge by the former—in French—meaning philosophy of science, even this meaning is characterized by ambiguity and generality, for every reflection on any science and any aspect of it, whether in its hypotheses, principles, laws, or results, is in one way or another a philosophy of that science. And before concluding the discussion on the relationship between theory of knowledge and epistemology, I should point out two opinions—in this regard—apparently contradictory within the same French meaning: The first opinion: Whatever is said about the existence of disagreement between theory of knowledge and epistemology, a close connection links them on the side of the general meaning of theory of knowledge that investigates its nature, limits, and the possibility of obtaining it, and on the side of the precise special meaning of epistemology as a prominent manifestation on the level of abstract philosophical analysis; that is, there is connection and separation between them, and there is greater generality for theory of knowledge and more precise specificity for epistemology. The second opinion: It builds its opinion on the basis of the distinction of theory of knowledge in all philosophical schools from epistemology, then concludes by saying: Epistemology is not a theory of knowledge; because it tries not to connect—like theory of knowledge—with the general goals of any philosophical system. And on the other hand, epistemology is not a part of theory of knowledge specific to research in practical knowledge, because the fundamental difference between theory of knowledge and epistemology is that epistemology has no goal of constructing a special theory of knowledge and that it deals with scientific thinking in a historical stage of stages.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion that distinguishes between two related fields: epistemology (الإبستمولوجيا) and theory of knowledge (نظرية المعرفة). The author begins by noting that in English, these terms are often used synonymously, but in French they are distinct. Following the French tradition, particularly philosopher Lalande, epistemology is defined as the philosophy of science—a critical study of the principles, hypotheses, and results of various sciences, aiming to determine their logical origin, value, and objective scope. It is not methodology, nor a speculative synthesis of scientific laws. Epistemology is an entry point and necessary aid to theory of knowledge, but differs by studying knowledge in detail and retrospectively across different sciences, rather than at the level of the unity of thought. The author then presents two contrasting opinions within the French framework. The first opinion holds that despite apparent disagreement, there is a close connection between theory of knowledge and epistemology: theory of knowledge has a general meaning (nature, limits, possibility of knowledge) while epistemology has a precise special meaning (abstract philosophical analysis of scientific knowledge). There is both connection and separation, with theory of knowledge being more general and epistemology more specific. The second opinion argues that epistemology is not a theory of knowledge at all, because it does not aim to build a special theory of knowledge nor tie itself to the general goals of any philosophical system. Its focus is on scientific thinking within a historical stage. The passage thus clarifies the French usage of these terms and highlights the nuances in their relationship.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion presented in the passage, why is epistemology not a theory of knowledge?
A) Because epistemology is broader and includes all sciences
B) Because epistemology avoids connecting to the general goals of any philosophical system
C) Because theory of knowledge is only concerned with practical knowledge
D) Because epistemology is part of theory of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The relationship between epistemology and theory of knowledge is debated even within the French tradition, with views ranging from complementary connection to complete independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary debate in Islamic studies about the relationship between Usul al-Fiqh and Maqasid al-Shariah. Frame it using the two opinions presented in this lesson: one that sees a close connection (general vs specific) and one that sees them as independent disciplines.
?
Write a short argument supporting one of the two opinions on the relationship between epistemology and theory of knowledge, using an example from Islamic science (e.g., whether the study of the Quranic worldview is a general theory of knowledge or a specific epistemological analysis).
